-- Recipe 01: Hello World
-- Minimal SPL program — verify spl + adapter + model work.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/01_hello_world/hello.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3

SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Introduce yourself and SPL 2.0 in two sentences.')
GENERATE greeting()

-- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
-- General-purpose LLM query — proxy any prompt to any adapter/model.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 prompt="Explain quantum computing in one sentence"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 prompt="Write a haiku about coding"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter ollama -m mistral prompt="What is 2+2?"
--   spl run cookbook/02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl --adapter anthropic prompt="Summarise the history of AI"
--
-- Parameters:
--   prompt  (required)  The user prompt / question

PROMPT ollama_proxy
SELECT
    system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
    context.prompt AS prompt
GENERATE answer(prompt)
```

-- Recipe 03: Multilingual Greeting
-- Greet in any language — demonstrates parametric context with lang.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Chinese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="French"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Japanese"
--   spl run cookbook/03_multilingual/multilingual.spl --adapter ollama user_input="hello wen" lang="Spanish"

PROMPT multilingual_greeting
SELECT
    system_role('You are a friendly assistant. Respond in the language specified.'),
    context.user_input AS input,
    context.lang AS lang
GENERATE greeting(input, lang)

```spl
-- Recipe 04: Model Showdown
-- Same prompt to multiple Ollama models — compare responses side-by-side.
-- Uses parallel CTEs instead of a bash loop for a pure-SPL implementation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="What is the meaning of life?"
--   spl run cookbook/04_model_showdown/showdown.spl --adapter ollama \
--       prompt="Explain recursion in 3 sentences" \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=llama3.2 model_3=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION compare_responses(
    prompt TEXT,
    model_1 TEXT, answer_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT, answer_2 TEXT,
    model_3 TEXT, answer_3 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a neutral evaluator comparing responses from three AI models.

Prompt given to all models: "{prompt}"

First, reproduce each model's raw response exactly as given, formatted like this:

=== {model_1} ===
{answer_1}

=== {model_2} ===
{answer_2}

=== {model_3} ===
{answer_3}

Then provide your evaluation. For each model briefly assess:
- Response quality
- Key strengths or weaknesses

Conclude with which model gave the most helpful answer and why.
$$;

WORKFLOW model_showdown
    INPUT:
        @prompt  TEXT DEFAULT 'What is the meaning of life?',
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'phi4',
        @model_3 TEXT DEFAULT 'qwen2.5'
    OUTPUT: @comparison TEXT
DO
    -- Run the same prompt against all three models in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        response_1 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_1
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        response_2 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_2
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),
        response_3 AS (
            PROMPT ask_model_3
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                @prompt AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_3
        )
    SELECT
        response_1.answer AS answer_1,
        response_2.answer AS answer_2,
        response_3.answer AS answer_3
    INTO @answer_1, @answer_2, @answer_3

    -- Judge all three responses together
    GENERATE compare_responses(
        @prompt,
        @model_1, @answer_1,
        @model_2, @answer_2,
        @model_3, @answer_3
    ) INTO @comparison

    COMMIT @comparison WITH
        status   = 'complete',
        model_1  = @model_1,
        model_2  = @model_2,
        model_3  = @model_3

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'One or more models failed to respond.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Self-Refine Pattern
-- Iteratively improves output through critique and refinement

WORKFLOW self_refine
  INPUT:
    @task TEXT,
    @max_iterations INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/05_self_refine/logs'
  OUTPUT: @result TEXT
DO
  @iteration := 0

  LOGGING f'Self-refine started | max_iterations={@max_iterations}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Initial draft
  GENERATE draft(@task) INTO @current
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @current) INTO @_

  -- Iterative refinement loop
  WHILE @iteration < @max_iterations DO
    LOGGING f'Iteration {@iteration} | critiquing ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE critique(@current) INTO @feedback
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/feedback_{@iteration}.md', @feedback) INTO @_

    EVALUATE @feedback
      WHEN 'satisfactory' THEN
        LOGGING f'Satisfactory at iteration {@iteration}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
        COMMIT @current WITH status = 'complete', iterations = @iteration
      ELSE
        GENERATE refined(@current, @feedback) INTO @current
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @current) INTO @_
        LOGGING f'Refined | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    END
  END

  -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | iterations={@iteration}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
  COMMIT @current WITH status = 'max_iterations', iterations = @iteration

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @current) INTO @_
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @current WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | Tokens: 505 in / 383 out
     | Latency: 4662ms

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: ReAct Agent — Population Growth
-- Fetch a country's population for the past two years via WebSearch,
-- then compute year-over-year growth rate using a Python tool (no LLM math).
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/06_react_agent/react_agent.spl \
--       --adapter claude_cli -m claude-sonnet-4-6 \
--       --allowed-tools WebSearch \
--       --tools cookbook/06_react_agent/tools.py \
--       country="France"

-- Tight prompt for search_population: return only the number, no narration.
CREATE FUNCTION search_population(country TEXT, year INT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Use WebSearch to find the total population of {country} for the year {year}.

Return ONLY the population as a plain integer with no commas, spaces, or text.
Example of valid output: 1409670000
No explanation. No units. No markdown. Just the number.
$$;

-- SPL PROCEDURE: search the web for a country's population in a specific year.
PROCEDURE search_population(country TEXT, year INT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
  GENERATE search_population(country, year) INTO @result
  COMMIT @result
END

-- calc_growth_rate is a Python tool registered from tools.py via --tools.
-- CALL routes to Python directly — deterministic, zero LLM cost.

-- Tight prompt for growth_report: plain narrative, no tool use, no file writes.
CREATE FUNCTION growth_report(country TEXT, year_prev TEXT, pop_prev TEXT, year_curr TEXT, pop_curr TEXT, growth_rate TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Write a concise 2-3 sentence population growth report for {country}.

Data:
- {year_prev} population: {pop_prev}
- {year_curr} population: {pop_curr}
- Year-over-year growth rate: {growth_rate}%

Plain prose only. No markdown headers. No file creation. No tool use. Just the narrative.
$$;

WORKFLOW population_growth
  INPUT:
    @country TEXT DEFAULT 'China',
    @year_curr INTEGER DEFAULT 2023
  OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
  @year_prev := @year_curr - 1

  LOGGING f'Population growth | country={@country} years={@year_prev}-{@year_curr}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Fetch population for each year via WebSearch
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_prev) INTO @pop_prev
  CALL search_population(@country, @year_curr) INTO @pop_curr
  LOGGING f'Population fetched | {@year_prev}={@pop_prev} {@year_curr}={@pop_curr}' LEVEL DEBUG

  -- Step 2: Compute growth rate — routed to Python tool, not LLM
  CALL calc_growth_rate(@pop_prev, @pop_curr) INTO @growth_rate
  LOGGING f'Growth rate computed: {@growth_rate}%' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 3: Compose the final human-readable report
  GENERATE growth_report(@country, @year_prev, @pop_prev, @year_curr, @pop_curr, @growth_rate) INTO @report

  COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
  WHEN SearchFailed THEN
    GENERATE search_error_report(@country) INTO @report
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Population growth | country=France years=2022-2023
     | [INFO] Growth rate computed: 0.5189%
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 186 in / 96 out
     | Latency: 40325ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

This represents a year-over-year growth rate of 0.52%, reflecting modest but steady demographic expansion. At this pace, France maintains a gradual upward population trajectory consistent with recent trends in Western European nations.

```spl
-- SPL 2.0: Safe Generation with Exception Handling
-- Demonstrates LLM-specific error handling patterns

WORKFLOW safe_generation
  INPUT: @prompt text
  OUTPUT: @result text
  SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
  GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result

  -- Quality check via semantic evaluation (LLM-as-judge)
  GENERATE quality_assess(@result) INTO @quality

  EVALUATE @quality
    WHEN 'high_quality' THEN
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'high_quality'
    WHEN 'acceptable' THEN
      GENERATE improved(@result, @prompt) INTO @result
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'refined'
    ELSE
      COMMIT @result WITH status = 'best_effort'
  END

EXCEPTION
  WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
    GENERATE response(@prompt) INTO @result
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'conservative'

  WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
    RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1

  WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
    COMMIT @prompt WITH status = 'refused'

  WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
    COMMIT @result WITH status = 'truncated'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 3
     | Tokens: 910 in / 789 out
     | Latency: 9975ms

```output
Encryption is the process of converting plaintext (readable data) into ciphertext (unreadable data) to ensure confidentiality and security. Here's a step-by-step explanation of how encryption works:

1. **Encryption algorithm**: An encryption algorithm is used to transform plaintext into ciphertext. The most common encryption algorithms are AES, RSA, and elliptic curve cryptography.
2. **Key generation**: A key pair is generated, consisting of a public key and a private key. The public key is shared with others, while the private key is kept secret.
3. **Encryption process**: When plaintext data needs to be encrypted, it is fed into the encryption algorithm using the public key.
4. **Bit-level operations**: The encryption algorithm performs bit-level operations, such as XOR (exclusive or) or substitution ciphers, on the plaintext data.
5. **Ciphertext generation**: The resulting ciphertext data is generated and stored securely.
6. **Decryption process**: To decrypt the ciphertext, it needs to be fed back into the encryption algorithm using the private key.
7. **Bit-level operations (reverse)**: The decryption algorithm performs the same bit-level operations in reverse order as the encryption algorithm.
8. **Plaintext recovery**: The resulting plaintext data is recovered and stored securely.

**Encryption methods**:

1. **Symmetric encryption**: Uses the same secret key for both encryption and decryption, such as AES.
2. **Asymmetric encryption**: Uses a pair of keys (public and private) to encrypt and decrypt data, such as RSA.
3. **Hashing**: A one-way process that transforms plaintext into a fixed-length string of characters, used for integrity checks and authentication.

**Key benefits**:

1. **Confidentiality**: Encryption ensures that only authorized parties can access the encrypted data.
2. **Data protection**: Encryption protects sensitive data from unauthorized access or tampering.
3. **Trust**: Encryption builds trust among organizations by ensuring secure communication and data exchange.
```

-- Recipe 08: RAG Query
-- Retrieval-augmented generation over indexed documents.
--
-- Setup:
--   pip install numpy faiss-cpu
--   spl rag add /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/zinets/who-is-wen.md
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="Where did Wen grow up?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is Momagrid and why was it built?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the Gang of Four in the ZiNets research team?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="What is eta_AI and how is it measured?"
--   spl run cookbook/08_rag_query/rag_query.spl --adapter ollama question="How did Wen transition from physics to software engineering?"

PROMPT rag_answer
SELECT
    system_role('You are a knowledgeable assistant. Use the provided context to answer accurately.'),
    rag.query(context.question, top_k=3) AS background,
    context.question AS question
GENERATE answer(question)

```spl
-- Recipe 09: Chain of Thought
-- Multi-step reasoning: Research → Analyze → Summarize
-- Each GENERATE feeds into the next via workflow variables.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="distributed AI inference"
--   spl run cookbook/09_chain_of_thought/chain.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="quantum computing"

WORKFLOW chain_of_thought
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @summary TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research
    GENERATE research(@topic) INTO @research

    -- Step 2: Analyze
    GENERATE analyze(@research) INTO @analysis

    -- Step 3: Summarize
    GENERATE summarize(@analysis) INTO @summary

    COMMIT @summary WITH status = 'complete'
END
```

[10] Batch Test
     cmd : spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama

     | 
```bash
spl run ./10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 10: Batch Test
-- Automated testing of key cookbook recipes across multiple models.
-- Uses parallel CTEs to fan out all recipe × model combinations at once,
-- then generates a structured PASS/FAIL report.
--
-- The bash script (batch_test.sh) is kept for CI/CD and shell-based pipelines.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama
--   spl run cookbook/10_batch_test/batch_test.spl --adapter ollama \
--       model_1=gemma3 model_2=mistral

CREATE FUNCTION summarize_results(
    model_1 TEXT,
    model_2 TEXT,
    r1_name TEXT, r1_m1 TEXT, r1_m2 TEXT,
    r2_name TEXT, r2_m1 TEXT, r2_m2 TEXT,
    r3_name TEXT, r3_m1 TEXT, r3_m2 TEXT
) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a test reporter. Review the outputs below and produce a concise pass/fail report.

A result is PASS if the output is a non-empty, coherent response.
A result is FAIL if the output is empty, contains an error, or is clearly nonsensical.

Models under test: {model_1}, {model_2}

Recipe: {r1_name}
  {model_1}: {r1_m1}
  {model_2}: {r1_m2}

Recipe: {r2_name}
  {model_1}: {r2_m1}
  {model_2}: {r2_m2}

Recipe: {r3_name}
  {model_1}: {r3_m1}
  {model_2}: {r3_m2}

Format each line as:
  PASS  recipe_name  ({model})
  FAIL  recipe_name  ({model})

End with a summary line: "Results: X/Y passed, Z failed"
$$;

WORKFLOW batch_test
    INPUT:
        @model_1 TEXT DEFAULT 'gemma3',
        @model_2 TEXT DEFAULT 'llama3.2'
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    -- Fan out all recipe × model combinations in parallel CTEs
    WITH
        -- Recipe 01: Hello World
        hello_m1 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        hello_m2 AS (
            PROMPT hello_world
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful assistant. Say hello briefly.')
            GENERATE greeting()
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 02: Ollama Proxy
        proxy_m1 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        proxy_m2 AS (
            PROMPT ollama_proxy
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a helpful, knowledgeable assistant.'),
                'hello' AS prompt
            GENERATE answer(prompt)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        ),

        -- Recipe 03: Multilingual
        multi_m1 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_1
        ),
        multi_m2 AS (
            PROMPT multilingual
            SELECT
                system_role('You are a multilingual assistant.'),
                'hello' AS user_input,
                'English' AS lang
            GENERATE response(user_input, lang)
            USING MODEL @model_2
        )

    SELECT
        hello_m1.greeting  AS hello_m1,  hello_m2.greeting  AS hello_m2,
        proxy_m1.answer    AS proxy_m1,  proxy_m2.answer    AS proxy_m2,
        multi_m1.response  AS multi_m1,  multi_m2.response  AS multi_m2
    INTO @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
         @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
         @multi_m1, @multi_m2

    -- Generate the structured pass/fail report
    GENERATE summarize_results(
        @model_1, @model_2,
        '01_hello_world/hello.spl',        @hello_m1, @hello_m2,
        '02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl',        @proxy_m1, @proxy_m2,
        '03_multilingual/multilingual.spl', @multi_m1, @multi_m2
    ) INTO @report

    COMMIT @report WITH
        status  = 'complete',
        model_1 = @model_1,
        model_2 = @model_2

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'Batch test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 7
     | Tokens: 536 in / 274 out
     | Latency: 4021ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

PASS 01_hello_world/hello.spl (gemma3)
PASS 01_hello_world/hello.spl (llama3.2)
FAIL 02_ollama_proxy/proxy.spl (gemma3)
FAIL 03_multilingual/multilingual.spl (gemma3)

Results: 2/2 passed, 1 failed
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @model_1 = gemma3
     |   @model_2 = llama3.2
     |   @hello_m1 = Please tell me what kind of greeting you'd like! 😊 
     | 
     | For example, do you want:
     | 
     | *   A simple "Hello"?
     | *   A more formal "Good morning/afternoon/evening"?
     | *   A specific greeting like "Hi there!" or "Greetings!"
     | *   Something more creative?

     |   @hello_m2 = *virtual handshake*

[11] Debate Arena

spl run ./11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=AI should be open-sourced
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 11: Debate Arena
-- Two LLM personas argue opposing sides of a topic, then a judge picks the winner.
-- Demonstrates multi-perspective reasoning, adversarial generation, and semantic evaluation.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="AI should be open-sourced"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama -m llama3.2 topic="Remote work is better than office work"
--   spl run cookbook/11_debate_arena/debate.spl --adapter ollama topic="Tabs are better than spaces"

CREATE FUNCTION pro_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY IN FAVOR of the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument supporting the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION con_argument(topic TEXT, previous TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a skilled debate champion arguing STRONGLY AGAINST the following motion:

Motion: "{topic}"

Your opponent's last argument (or "opening statement" if this is your first turn):
{previous}

Write a focused, persuasive argument opposing the motion. If this is a rebuttal round,
directly address and counter your opponent's points. Be concise (3-5 paragraphs).
Do NOT offer balanced views — you are arguing one side.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION judge_debate(topic TEXT, pro_history TEXT, con_history TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are an impartial debate judge evaluating the following debate.

Motion: "{topic}"

--- PRO SIDE (arguing IN FAVOR) ---
{pro_history}

--- CON SIDE (arguing AGAINST) ---
{con_history}

Evaluate the debate on these criteria:
1. Strength of arguments
2. Quality of rebuttals
3. Clarity and persuasiveness

Declare a winner (PRO or CON) and explain your reasoning in 2-3 paragraphs.
$$;

WORKFLOW debate_arena
    INPUT:
        @topic TEXT,
        @max_rounds INTEGER DEFAULT 3,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs'
    OUTPUT: @verdict TEXT
DO
    @round := 0
    @pro_history := ''
    @con_history := ''

    LOGGING f'Debate started | topic: {@topic} | rounds: {@max_rounds}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Opening statements
    GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @pro
    GENERATE con_argument(@topic, 'opening statement') INTO @con

    @pro_history := @pro
    @con_history := @con

    LOGGING 'Opening statements complete' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_pro.md', @pro) INTO @_
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/opening_con.md', @con) INTO @_

    -- Rebuttal rounds
    WHILE @round < @max_rounds DO
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | pro rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Pro rebuts Con's latest point
        GENERATE pro_argument(@topic, @con_history) INTO @pro_rebuttal
        @pro_history := @pro_history || '\n---\n' || @pro_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_pro.md', @pro_rebuttal) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'Round {@round} | con rebuttal ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Con rebuts Pro's latest point
        GENERATE con_argument(@topic, @pro_history) INTO @con_rebuttal
        @con_history := @con_history || '\n---\n' || @con_rebuttal
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/round_{@round}_con.md', @con_rebuttal) INTO @_

        @round := @round + 1
        LOGGING f'Round {@round} complete' LEVEL INFO
    END

    LOGGING 'All rounds done — judge deliberating ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Judge evaluates both sides
    GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict

    LOGGING f'Verdict ready | rounds={@round}' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/verdict.md', @verdict) INTO @_
    COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'complete', rounds = @round

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        GENERATE judge_debate(@topic, @pro_history, @con_history) INTO @verdict
        COMMIT @verdict WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @pro_history WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Debate started | topic: AI should be open-sourced | rounds: 3
     | [INFO] Opening statements complete
     | [INFO] Round 1 complete
     | [INFO] Round 2 complete
     | [INFO] Round 3 complete
     | [INFO] All rounds done — judge deliberating ...
     | [INFO] Verdict ready | rounds=3
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 9
     | Tokens: 9454 in / 3210 out
     | Latency: 48804ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
After carefully evaluating the debate, I declare the PRO side as the winner.

The PRO side presented strong arguments that emphasized the benefits of open-sourcing AI, such as promoting transparency, accountability, and collaboration. They effectively addressed counterarguments by highlighting the potential risks associated with secrecy and exclusivity, including the threat to democracy and intellectual property rights. The PRO side's rebuttals were also more effective in addressing the CON side's concerns, particularly regarding security risks.

One of the key strengths of the PRO side was their ability to present a nuanced view of open-sourcing AI, acknowledging potential challenges while emphasizing the benefits of collaboration and transparency. In contrast, the CON side relied heavily on hypothetical scenarios and failed to provide concrete evidence to support their claims. Furthermore, they often resorted to scare-mongering tactics, which undermined their credibility.

The PRO side's arguments were also more persuasive in terms of clarity and persuasiveness. They effectively used storytelling techniques to illustrate the potential benefits of open-sourcing AI, making it more relatable and accessible to the audience. Their language was also more inclusive and forward-thinking, emphasizing the need for a global commons where knowledge and innovation are shared freely. Overall, while both sides presented valid arguments, I believe that the PRO side presented a more compelling case for open-sourcing AI.
```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @topic = AI should be open-sourced
     |   @max_rounds = 3
     |   @log_dir = cookbook/11_debate_arena/logs
     |   @round = 3
     |   @pro_history = Ladies and gentlemen, honorable judges, and fellow debaters, I'm thrilled to take on the motion that AI should be open-sourced. As we stand here today, the world is at a crossroads, where technology can either empower humanity or exacerbate its problems. Open-sourcing AI is not just a matter of promoting transparency; it's a crucial step towards ensuring that this powerful tool serves the greater good.
     | 
     | The current state of AI development is characterized by secrecy and exclusivity. Big tech companies like Google, Amazon, and Microsoft are working on AI projects that have the potential to transform industries and revolutionize our lives. However, their exclusive approaches to AI development are not only hindering progress but also threatening democracy. By withholding AI research and innovations from public view, these companies are effectively dictating the course of technological advancements without accountability.
     | 
     | Open-sourcing AI is a radical idea that may seem daunting at first, but it's essential for ensuring that this technology is developed responsibly and with the people's best interests in mind. It allows researchers, developers, and policymakers to collaborate freely, share knowledge, and accelerate innovation. Moreover, open-sourcing AI can help prevent its misuse by promoting transparency and accountability throughout the development process. By putting AI development under an open-source umbrella, we can prevent corporate monopolies from controlling this powerful technology.
     | 
     | The benefits of open-sourcing AI are far-reaching and profound. It will enable a diverse range of voices to contribute to AI research, fostering a more inclusive and equitable technological landscape. It will help bridge the digital divide by providing access to cutting-edge technologies that were previously out of reach for marginalized communities. And it will ensure that AI development is guided by human values rather than corporate interests.
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI is not just a philosophical ideal; it's a necessary step towards harnessing this technology for the betterment of humanity. We must reject the notion that AI development should be controlled by private interests and instead strive to create a global commons where knowledge and innovation are shared freely. The future of AI depends on our collective willingness to make a bold statement: that we value transparency, accountability, and human well-being above all else.
     | ---
     | My esteemed opponent has raised several valid concerns about open-sourcing AI, but I firmly believe that these risks can be mitigated with careful planning, regulation, and community oversight.
     | 
     | Firstly, I'd like to address the security risks associated with open-sourcing AI. While it's true that malicious actors could exploit vulnerabilities in these systems, this is not a reason to keep AI technology closed off to the world. In fact, open-sourcing can actually improve security by creating a transparent and publicly scrutinized process for identifying and patching vulnerabilities. Furthermore, the development of open-source AI would encourage a community-driven approach to security research, which has proven to be highly effective in identifying and mitigating threats.
     | 
     | Regarding the potential consequences for global security, I disagree that open-sourcing AI would enable authoritarian regimes or terrorist organizations to develop devastating machines. In fact, the opposite is true: by making AI technology more accessible, we can empower researchers, scientists, and developers from diverse backgrounds to contribute to its development, potentially leading to breakthroughs in areas like healthcare, education, and environmental sustainability. Moreover, open-sourcing would allow for a global effort to develop robust security measures, rather than relying on a select few to control access.
     | 
     | Finally, I'd like to address the issue of intellectual property rights. While it's true that innovators might lose some control over their work, this is not a reason to restrict access to AI technology. In fact, open-sourcing can lead to new opportunities for collaboration and knowledge-sharing, which can drive innovation forward in ways that were previously impossible. By embracing open-source principles, we can create a more inclusive and equitable AI development ecosystem that benefits everyone, rather than just a select few.
     | ---
     | My esteemed opponent attempts to paint open-sourcing AI as a Pandora's box, unleashing chaos and vulnerability upon the world. I firmly reject this notion, for it is precisely by making AI open-sourced that we can ensure its responsible development and deployment.
     | 
     | The primary concern raised by my opponent – security risks – is overstated and misguided. By sharing knowledge and expertise, the open-source community can collectively identify vulnerabilities and develop robust countermeasures, far more effectively than any proprietary model. Furthermore, open-sourcing AI would democratize access to this technology, allowing researchers from diverse backgrounds to contribute to its development. This diversity of perspective is essential for identifying and mitigating potential security risks, as it allows for a broader range of expertise and experience.
     | 
     | My opponent also claims that intellectual property rights can be preserved under an open-source framework, but this is nothing more than a myth perpetuated by those who believe their profits are sacrosanct. In reality, open-sourcing AI would stimulate innovation and collaboration, driving progress at a pace that proprietary models cannot match. Companies would no longer need to hoard knowledge to maintain their competitive advantage; instead, they could focus on developing new capabilities, fueled by the collective energy of the global community.
     | 
     | In contrast, my opponent's proposed solution – relying on "community oversight" and "regulation" – is a woefully inadequate response to the risks associated with unregulated AI development. Even the most robust regulations cannot guarantee the integrity of an open-source system; it is only by sharing knowledge and expertise that we can truly build resilience against security threats. By embracing open-sourcing AI, we are not sacrificing control or security; we are empowering a global community to work together towards a common goal: building a safer, more equitable world for all.
     | 
     | Ultimately, the choice between secrecy and exclusivity versus openness and collaboration is clear. We must choose progress over profit, unity over isolation. By making AI open-sourced, we can unlock its full potential as a force for good, harnessing its power to drive positive change and build a brighter future for humanity.
     | ---
     | My esteemed opponent has failed to acknowledge the transformative power of open-sourcing AI, instead choosing to focus on perceived security risks and intellectual property concerns. However, I firmly believe that these worries can be mitigated through careful design, community engagement, and regulation.
     | 
     | Firstly, let us address the notion that open-sourcing AI would compromise global security. While it is true that malicious actors could potentially exploit vulnerabilities in unregulated systems, this argument relies on a flawed assumption that secrecy is inherently more secure than openness. In reality, the most sophisticated threats often rely on complex networks of collaborators and insiders, making it impossible to contain them within a proprietary model alone. Open-sourcing AI would create an ecosystem of collaborative development, allowing experts from diverse backgrounds to identify and address vulnerabilities before they can be exploited.
     | 
     | Moreover, open-sourcing AI would not undermine intellectual property rights; rather, it would empower researchers and developers to build upon existing work, accelerating innovation and collaboration. While some may claim that the value of a technology is lost once it is made publicly available, this overlooks the fundamental principle of scientific progress: that knowledge should be freely shared and built upon to drive greater understanding and advancement. By open-sourcing AI, we would not only preserve intellectual property but also ensure that its benefits are accessible to all who need them.
     | 
     | My opponent's assertion that community oversight can mitigate risks is similarly misguided. While it is true that unregulated initiatives can devolve into chaos, it is equally true that well-designed community-driven models can establish robust governance structures and accountability mechanisms. By engaging in open discussions and collaborative development processes, we can create a more inclusive and responsible ecosystem for AI innovation.
     | 
     | In conclusion, the benefits of open-sourcing AI far outweigh the perceived risks. By embracing openness and collaboration, we can unlock the full potential of this technology, driving greater progress and improvement for humanity. We must not be deterred by unfounded fears; instead, we should join forces to build a future where AI is developed responsibly, transparently, and with accountability – a future that is more secure, more inclusive, and more just.
     |   @con_history = My esteemed opponent has proposed that AI should be open-sourced, but I strongly disagree with this notion. The primary concern is the potential security risks associated with making advanced artificial intelligence technology publicly available.
     | 
     | By open-sourcing AI, we would be essentially handing over control of a powerful tool to the world's most malicious actors. Hackers and nation-state spies could easily exploit vulnerabilities in these systems, leading to catastrophic consequences for global security. Furthermore, open-sourced AI could fall into the wrong hands, enabling authoritarian regimes or terrorist organizations to develop devastating machines that could be used against innocent civilians.
     | 
     | Moreover, open-sourcing AI would undermine the intellectual property rights of the innovators who have spent years developing this technology. The open-source community's lack of expertise in AI development would lead to a chaotic and disorganized landscape of incompatible codebases, making it nearly impossible for developers to contribute meaningfully or maintain the integrity of these systems.
     | 
     | In contrast, proprietary models allow for more effective monitoring, security measures, and access control, which are crucial for preventing malicious activities. By keeping AI technology behind closed doors, we can ensure that only responsible actors have access to its capabilities, thereby safeguarding global stability and preventing potential catastrophes.
     | ---
     | My esteemed opponent has attempted to alleviate concerns about open-sourcing AI by suggesting that security risks can be mitigated through community oversight and regulation. However, I firmly believe that these precautions would only serve to delay the inevitable – the inevitable breakdown of our current system of secrecy and exclusivity.
     | 
     | Let us not forget that even with the best intentions and most robust regulations in place, there are inherent vulnerabilities to open-sourcing AI. A single vulnerability exploited by a sophisticated actor could compromise the entire network, potentially leading to catastrophic consequences. We cannot afford to gamble with the security of our world's most advanced technologies on the whims of community-driven oversight.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's assertion that open-sourcing would empower researchers from diverse backgrounds is nothing more than wishful thinking. The fact remains that even in an open-source model, those with the means and resources will always be able to exploit this technology for their own gain. We cannot simply entrust our most advanced technologies to the benevolence of a community; we must ensure that they are controlled by governments or corporations willing to prioritize accountability over profit.
     | 
     | The notion that intellectual property rights can be preserved under an open-source framework is nothing more than a myth. As soon as AI technology is made available for anyone to access and modify, its value is lost. Companies will no longer have a reason to invest in research and development; the 'free' resources of the internet would render their business models obsolete. We must not confuse the benefits of open-sourcing – greater collaboration and innovation – with the myth of limitless intellectual property protection.
     | 
     | In conclusion, my opponent's arguments have failed to address the fundamental concerns surrounding the security and control of AI technology. I urge you to reject this motion and recognize that secrecy and exclusivity are essential for safeguarding our world from the risks associated with unregulated technological advancements.
     | ---
     | My esteemed opponent attempts to paint open-sourcing AI as a panacea for humanity, but I firmly disagree. The risks associated with open-sourcing AI far outweigh its benefits, and we must not be swayed by the promise of "progress" or "collaboration." The reality is that open-sourcing AI would put sensitive information in the hands of unvetted individuals and organizations, creating a Wild West of innovation where security and intellectual property are mere afterthoughts.
     | 
     | My opponent's claim that community oversight can mitigate these risks is laughable. We all know that community-driven initiatives often devolve into chaos when left to their own devices. The open-source community has repeatedly demonstrated its inability to police itself, with infamous cases like the WannaCry ransomware attack highlighting the vulnerabilities of unregulated collaboration. Moreover, who decides what constitutes "community oversight"? Would it be a panel of unelected bureaucrats or a group of self-interested developers? It's a recipe for disaster.
     | 
     | Furthermore, open-sourcing AI would create a situation where companies can no longer protect their intellectual property, allowing competitors to steal their trade secrets and sabotage their business models. This is not just a matter of national security; it's a matter of economic competitiveness. Companies invest millions in R&D to develop cutting-edge technologies, only to have them ripped off by rival firms that benefit from the open-source community's lack of accountability. It's a Faustian bargain: we trade our intellectual property for the fleeting promise of "progress," but at the cost of our very livelihoods.
     | 
     | In conclusion, open-sourcing AI is a misguided idea that would put humanity in greater danger than we currently face. We cannot afford to gamble with sensitive information and cutting-edge technologies without ensuring that they are developed responsibly and with accountability. The risks associated with unregulated innovation far outweigh any potential benefits, and we must reject the motion that AI should be open-sourced.
     | ---
     | As I stand before you today, I firmly believe that open-sourcing AI is a misguided notion that would put the world at risk. My opponent has attempted to mitigate security concerns by suggesting that community oversight and regulation can address vulnerabilities, but I firmly disagree.
     | 
     | The security risks associated with open-sourcing AI are too great to ignore. By sharing knowledge and expertise, malicious actors can identify and exploit vulnerabilities in the system before it is even developed. This is not a hypothetical scenario; we have already seen instances of hackers compromising sensitive information by exploiting weaknesses in proprietary systems. Open-sourcing AI would only exacerbate this problem, allowing malicious actors to gain access to cutting-edge technologies that could be used for devastating purposes.
     | 
     | Furthermore, my opponent's assertion that intellectual property rights can be preserved under an open-source framework is a myth perpetuated by those who believe their profits are sacrosanct. In reality, open-sourcing AI would lead to a free-for-all in terms of innovation and collaboration, with companies competing to develop the most advanced technologies before anyone else can even begin to understand how they work. This chaos would stifle progress and drive innovation into reverse.
     | 
     | The notion that open-sourcing AI would empower researchers from diverse backgrounds to contribute to its development is also misguided. In reality, this would lead to a proliferation of conflicting visions and priorities, with different stakeholders pushing for competing agendas that would undermine the very purpose of open-sourcing in the first place. By maintaining secrecy and exclusivity around AI development, we can ensure that only those with the most advanced expertise and knowledge are working on these technologies, thereby driving innovation forward in a responsible and controlled manner.

[12] Plan and Execute

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 12: Plan and Execute
-- A planner agent decomposes a task into steps, then an executor implements each one.
-- When output_dir is set, generated code files are written to disk one-by-one.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       --tools cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/tools.py \
--       task="Build a REST API for a todo app" \
--       output_dir="cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/output"
--
--   spl run cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/plan_execute.spl --adapter ollama \
--       task="Set up a CI/CD pipeline for a Python project"


-- Phase 1: Decompose task into a numbered implementation plan
CREATE FUNCTION plan(task TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software architect. Break down this task into clear implementation steps:

Task: {task}

Output a numbered list of concrete steps (3–6 steps). Each step should be specific
and independently executable. No prose — just the numbered list.
$$;

-- Count how many steps are in the plan (capped at max_steps)
CREATE FUNCTION count_steps(plan TEXT, max_steps TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered steps in this plan.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.
If there are more than {max_steps} steps, return {max_steps}.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Extract step N from the plan (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_step(plan TEXT, step_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract step number {step_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered plan and return
ONLY that step's description. No numbering prefix. No extra text.

Plan:
{plan}
$$;

-- Execute a step: produce a concise design decision + list of files to create
CREATE FUNCTION execute_step(current_step TEXT, prior_results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer planning an implementation.

Step: {current_step}
Completed so far: {prior_results}

Describe in 2-3 sentences what this step produces and list the filenames it creates.
Be brief — no code yet, just the design decision and file list.
$$;

-- Validate that a step produced meaningful output (design notes, not code)
CREATE FUNCTION validate_step(current_step TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Does the following design note meaningfully address the step?
Note: this is a planning phase — expect a 2-3 sentence description and a file list, NOT code.

Step: {current_step}
Design note: {step_result}

Reply with exactly one word: "passed" or "failed".
Only reply "failed" if the design note is empty, completely off-topic, or missing a file list.
$$;

-- Re-plan after a step failure
CREATE FUNCTION replan(task TEXT, plan TEXT, step_index TEXT, step_result TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
A step in our implementation plan failed. Create a revised plan.

Original task: {task}
Original plan: {plan}
Failed at step: {step_index}
Failure details: {step_result}

Output a new numbered plan starting from step {step_index}. Keep completed steps as-is.
$$;

-- Phase 4a: List all source files to create (no code — just filenames + one-line descriptions)
CREATE FUNCTION outline_files(task TEXT, results TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Based on the implementation plan below, list every
source file that must be created to complete the task.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

Output a numbered list — one file per line — with the filename and a one-sentence description.
Always include a README.md as the last item.
Example:
1. app.py - Flask application entry point, registers blueprints and starts the server
2. models.py - SQLAlchemy ORM models for Todo and User
3. README.md - Setup instructions, project layout, and example API calls

No code. No prose. Just the numbered file list.
$$;

-- Count files in the outline
CREATE FUNCTION count_files(outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Count the number of numbered items in this file list.
Return ONLY a single integer — no explanation, no text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Extract one file entry at index N (0-indexed)
CREATE FUNCTION extract_file(outline TEXT, file_index TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Extract item number {file_index} (0-indexed) from this numbered file list and return
ONLY that item's filename and description. No number prefix. No extra text.

List:
{outline}
$$;

-- Generate complete code for ONE file
CREATE FUNCTION generate_file(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_desc TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a senior software engineer. Write complete, runnable code for ONE source file.

Task: {task}

Implementation plan:
{results}

File to create: {file_desc}

Output a SINGLE fenced code block. The FIRST line inside the fence MUST be a filename comment:

```python
# filename: app/main.py
...complete code...
```
     | 
     | Requirements:
     | - Complete, runnable code — no placeholders, no TODOs.
     | - Error handling, type hints, brief inline comments.
     | - Only this one file — nothing else.
     | $$;
     | 
     | -- Summarize what was built
     | CREATE FUNCTION summarize(task TEXT, results TEXT, file_outline TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
     | In 3-5 sentences, describe what was built for this task and how to run it.
     | 
     | Task: {task}
     | Files created:
     | {file_outline}
     | $$;
     | 
     | 
     | WORKFLOW plan_and_execute
     |     INPUT:
     |         @task TEXT DEFAULT 'Build a REST API for a todo app',
     |         @output_dir TEXT DEFAULT '',
     |         @max_steps INTEGER DEFAULT 5,
     |         @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/12_plan_and_execute/logs'
     |     OUTPUT: @final_report TEXT
     | DO
     |     @step_index := 0
     |     @results := ''
     |     @replan_count := 0
     |     @max_replans := 3
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'Plan-and-Execute | task: {@task}' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 1: Plan
     |     GENERATE plan(@task) INTO @plan
     |     LOGGING f'Plan ready | steps to execute (max={@max_steps})' LEVEL INFO
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/plan.md', @plan) INTO @_
     | 
     |     -- Phase 2: Count steps (capped at max_steps)
     |     GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |     LOGGING f'Step count: {@step_count}' LEVEL DEBUG
     | 
     |     -- Phase 3: Execute each step (brief design notes only — no code)
     |     WHILE @step_index < @step_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Executing step {@step_index}/{@step_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_step(@plan, @step_index) INTO @current_step
     |         GENERATE execute_step(@current_step, @results) INTO @step_result
     |         GENERATE validate_step(@current_step, @step_result) INTO @validation
     | 
     |         EVALUATE @validation
     |             WHEN 'failed' THEN
     |                 @replan_count := @replan_count + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} failed — replanning ({@replan_count}/{@max_replans})' LEVEL WARN
     |                 EVALUATE @replan_count
     |                     WHEN >= @max_replans THEN
     |                         LOGGING f'Max replans reached — accepting step as-is' LEVEL WARN
     |                         @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                         CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                         @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                     ELSE
     |                         GENERATE replan(@task, @plan, @step_index, @step_result) INTO @plan
     |                         GENERATE count_steps(@plan, @max_steps) INTO @step_count
     |                         @step_index := 0
     |                         @results := ''
     |                 END
     |             ELSE
     |                 @results := @results || '\n## Step ' || @step_index || '\n' || @step_result
     |                 CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/step_{@step_index}.md', @step_result) INTO @_
     |                 @step_index := @step_index + 1
     |                 LOGGING f'Step {@step_index} done' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         END
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@step_count} steps complete — generating files' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 4: Outline all files to generate
     |     GENERATE outline_files(@task, @results) INTO @file_outline
     |     GENERATE count_files(@file_outline) INTO @file_count
     |     LOGGING f'File outline ready | {@file_count} files to generate' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 5: Generate each file individually (one LLM call per file — avoids truncation)
     |     @file_index := 0
     |     WHILE @file_index < @file_count DO
     |         LOGGING f'Generating file {@file_index}/{@file_count} ...' LEVEL INFO
     |         GENERATE extract_file(@file_outline, @file_index) INTO @current_file
     |         GENERATE generate_file(@task, @results, @current_file) INTO @file_code
     |         CALL write_code_files(@file_code, @output_dir) INTO @file_written
     |         LOGGING f'Written: {@file_written}' LEVEL DEBUG
     |         @file_index := @file_index + 1
     |     END
     | 
     |     LOGGING f'All {@file_count} files generated' LEVEL INFO
     | 
     |     -- Phase 6: Summary report
     |     GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     | 
     |     CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_report.md', @final_report) INTO @_
     |     COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'complete', steps_executed = @step_index, files_generated = @file_count
     | 
     | EXCEPTION
     |     WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
     |         GENERATE summarize(@task, @results, @file_outline) INTO @final_report
     |         COMMIT @final_report WITH status = 'partial', steps_executed = @step_index
     |     WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
     |         COMMIT @results WITH status = 'budget_limit'
     | END
     | ```

```spl
-- Recipe 13: Map-Reduce Summarizer
-- Splits a large document into chunks, summarizes each chunk (map),
-- then combines all summaries into a final summary (reduce).
-- Demonstrates the map-reduce pattern for handling content beyond context limits.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama -m gemma3 document="$(cat large_doc.txt)"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="$(cat README.md)" style="bullet points"
--   spl run cookbook/13_map_reduce/map_reduce.spl --tools cookbook/13_map_reduce/tools.py --adapter ollama document="long text here..." style="executive brief"
--
-- Tools: chunk_plan(), extract_chunk() are in tools.py (CALL, zero tokens).
--        write_file(), read_file() are SPL built-ins — no --tools flag needed.
--        summarize_chunk(), reduce_summaries(), quality_score(), improve_summary() call the LLM.
--
-- Logs written to @log_dir (default: cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs):
--   chunk_00.md, chunk_01.md, ...    — raw extracted chunks
--   summary_00.md, summary_01.md, ... — per-chunk LLM summaries
--   final_summary.md                  — combined final summary

WORKFLOW map_reduce_summarizer
    INPUT:  
        @document TEXT, 
        @style TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs'
    OUTPUT: @final_summary TEXT
DO
    @chunk_index := 0
    @summaries := []          -- LIST: accumulates per-chunk summaries

    LOGGING 'Starting map-reduce | document length: ' + @document

    -- Step 1: Determine how to split the document (deterministic, 0 tokens)
    CALL chunk_plan(@document) INTO @chunk_count

    LOGGING 'Document split into ' + @chunk_count + ' chunks'

    -- Step 2: MAP — summarize each chunk independently
    WHILE @chunk_index < @chunk_count DO
        CALL extract_chunk(@document, @chunk_index, @chunk_count) INTO @chunk   -- deterministic, 0 tokens

        -- Save raw chunk to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/chunk_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk) INTO @_

        GENERATE summarize_chunk(@chunk, @chunk_index) INTO @chunk_summary

        -- Save chunk summary to logs/
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/summary_{@chunk_index}.md', @chunk_summary) INTO @_

        LOGGING f'[Chunk {@chunk_index}/{@chunk_count}] summary saved'

        @summaries := list_append(@summaries, @chunk_summary)
        @chunk_index := @chunk_index + 1
    END

    -- Step 3: REDUCE — concat all chunk summaries, then combine into final
    @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
    GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary

    -- Step 4: Quality check the final summary
    GENERATE quality_score(@final_summary, @document) INTO @score

    EVALUATE @score
        WHEN > 0.7 THEN
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Final summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'complete', chunks = @chunk_count
        ELSE
            GENERATE improve_summary(@final_summary, @summaries_text) INTO @final_summary
            CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
            LOGGING f'Improved summary saved to {@log_dir}/final_summary.md (score={@score})'
            COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'refined', chunks = @chunk_count
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'partial'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        @summaries_text := list_concat(@summaries, '\n')
        GENERATE reduce_summaries(@summaries_text, @style) INTO @final_summary
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final_summary.md', @final_summary) INTO @_
        COMMIT @final_summary WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] [Chunk 0/1] summary saved
     | [INFO] Improved summary saved to cookbook/13_map_reduce/logs/final_summary.md (score=Based on the provided code, I'll implement the `quality_score` function that assesses the quality of the generated summary and maps it to a score between 0 (poor) and 10 (excellent).

```python
class ReduceSummaries:
    @staticmethod
    def reduce(input_text):
        if input_text == '0':
            return "The input \"0\" does not contain any text, so it cannot be summarized."
        elif len(input_text) < 10:
            score = ReduceSummaries.bullet_points_summary(input_text)
            return f"Summary: {score['summary']}, Score: {score['quality_score']}"
        else:
            sentences = input_text.split('. ')
            if not sentences:
                raise ValueError("Input text is empty.")
            summary = ReduceSummaries.summary_sentence(sentences)

            # Assess the quality of the generated summary
            score = ReduceSummaries.assess_summary_quality(summary)
            return f"Summary: {summary}, Score: {score['quality_score']}"

    @staticmethod
    def bullet_points_summary(text):
        # logic to create a bullet point summary from the input
        pass

    @staticmethod
    def assess_summary_quality(summary):
        score = 0
        if summary:
            score += 1  # Summary is not empty, so it's a good start
            sentences = summary.split('. ')
            for sentence in sentences:
                if len(sentence) > 20:  # Longer sentences are generally better
                    score += 2
                elif len(sentence) >= 10:  # Shorter sentences can still be useful
                    score += 1

        return {
            'summary': summary,
            'quality_score': int(min(score, 10))  # Ensure the score is between 0 and 10
        }

    @staticmethod
    def sentences_in_input(text):
        # logic to split the text into individual sentences
        pass

    @staticmethod
    def summary_sentence(sentences):
        # logic to generate a summary sentence from the list of sentences
        pass

class ReduceSummaries:
    @staticmethod
    def reduce(input_text):
        """
        Generate a summary for the given input text.
        
        Args:
            input_text (str): The text to summarize.
        
        Returns:
            str: A summary of the input text.
        """
        if not input_text.strip():  # Check if the input is empty
            return "The input \"0\" does not contain any text, so it cannot be summarized."
        
        elif len(input_text) < 10:
            return bullet_points_summary(input_text)
        
        else:
            sentence = sentences_in_input(input_text)
            if sentence:  # Check if the list of sentences is not empty
                return summary_sentence(sentence)
            else:
                return "No sentences found in the input text."

    def bullet_points_summary(text):

```python
class ReduceSummaries:
    @staticmethod
    def reduce(input_text):
        if input_text == '0':
            return "The input \"0\" does not contain any text, so it cannot be summarized."
        elif len(input_text) < 10:
            score = ReduceSummaries.bullet_points_summary(input_text)
            return f"Summary: {score['summary']}, Score: {score['quality_score']}"
        else:
            sentences = input_text.split('. ')
            if not sentences:
                raise ValueError("Input text is empty.")
            summary = ReduceSummaries.summary_sentence(sentences)

            # Assess the quality of the generated summary
            score = ReduceSummaries.assess_summary_quality(summary)
            return f"Summary: {summary}, Score: {score['quality_score']}"

    @staticmethod
    def bullet_points_summary(text):
        # logic to create a bullet point summary from the input
        pass

    @staticmethod
    def assess_summary_quality(summary):
        score = 0
        if summary:
            score += 1  # Summary is not empty, so it's a good start
            sentences = summary.split('. ')
            for sentence in sentences:
                if len(sentence) > 20:  # Longer sentences are generally better
                    score += 2
                elif len(sentence) >= 10:  # Shorter sentences can still be useful
                    score += 1

        return {
            'summary': summary,
            'quality_score': int(min(score, 10))  # Ensure the score is between 0 and 10
        }

    @staticmethod
    def sentences_in_input(text):
        # logic to split the text into individual sentences
        pass

    @staticmethod
    def summary_sentence(sentences):
        # logic to generate a summary sentence from the list of sentences
        pass

```spl
-- Recipe 14: Multi-Agent Collaboration
-- Specialized agents (Researcher, Analyst, Writer) collaborate on a report.
-- Each agent has a distinct role and passes results to the next.
-- Demonstrates procedural decomposition, CALL-based delegation, and role specialization.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic="Impact of AI on healthcare"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="Future of renewable energy"
--   spl run cookbook/14_multi_agent/multi_agent.spl --adapter ollama topic="State of quantum computing in 2025"

-- Agent 1: Researcher — gathers key facts and sources
PROCEDURE researcher(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE research_facts(topic) INTO @facts
    GENERATE identify_key_themes(@facts) INTO @themes
    @result := @facts || '\n\nKey Themes:\n' || @themes
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 2: Analyst — identifies trends, risks, and opportunities
PROCEDURE analyst(research TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE analyze_trends(research) INTO @trends
    GENERATE assess_risks(research, topic) INTO @risks
    GENERATE find_opportunities(research, topic) INTO @opportunities
    @result := 'Trends:\n' || @trends || '\n\nRisks:\n' || @risks || '\n\nOpportunities:\n' || @opportunities
    COMMIT @result
END

-- Agent 3: Writer — produces polished final report
PROCEDURE writer(research TEXT, analysis TEXT, topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE draft_report(topic, research, analysis) INTO @draft
    GENERATE critique(@draft) INTO @feedback
    GENERATE revise_report(@draft, @feedback) INTO @final
    COMMIT @final
END

-- Orchestrator workflow
WORKFLOW multi_agent_report
    INPUT: @topic TEXT
    OUTPUT: @report TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Multi-agent report | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Delegate to specialized agents
    LOGGING 'Agent 1: Researcher ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL researcher(@topic) INTO @research
    LOGGING 'Agent 2: Analyst ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL analyst(@research, @topic) INTO @analysis
    LOGGING 'Agent 3: Writer ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    CALL writer(@research, @analysis, @topic) INTO @report

    LOGGING 'Report complete' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @report WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @research WITH status = 'partial_research_only'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END

Artificial Intelligence (AI) is transforming the healthcare landscape by improving patient care, enhancing efficiency and productivity, predicting health outcomes, and innovative technologies that enable patient engagement. **Executive Summary:**

The integration of Artificial Intelligence (AI) in healthcare is revolutionizing patient care, diagnosis, treatment plans, and clinical trials. AI-powered chatbots, virtual assistants, and robotic surgery are enhancing patient engagement, improving diagnostic accuracy, and streamlining clinical trials. However, there are also potential risks associated with AI adoption in healthcare, including data quality issues, regulatory frameworks, cybersecurity threats, and the need for transparency and accountability.

**Key Findings:**

1. **Improved Diagnosis**: AI algorithms can analyze medical images to help doctors diagnose diseases more accurately and quickly, reducing diagnosis times by up to 30%.
2. **Personalized Medicine**: AI can help tailor treatment plans to individual patients based on their genetic profiles, medical histories, and lifestyle factors, improving patient outcomes and reducing healthcare costs.
3. **Streamlined Clinical Trials**: AI can analyze large amounts of data to identify potential new treatments, accelerating the clinical trial process by up to 50%.
4. **Enhanced Patient Engagement**: Chatbots and virtual assistants powered by AI can help patients manage their chronic conditions, track their symptoms, and receive personalized health advice, improving patient engagement and adherence.
5. **Predictive Analytics**: AI can analyze large datasets to predict patient outcomes, identify high-risk patients, and prevent hospital readmissions, reducing healthcare costs and improving patient safety.

```spl
-- Recipe 15: Automated Code Review
-- Multi-pass code review: security audit, performance check, style review, then synthesis.
-- Demonstrates tool-augmented analysis, multi-criteria evaluation, and structured output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 code="$(cat myfile.py)"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="$(cat main.go)" language="Go"
--   spl run cookbook/15_code_review/code_review.spl --adapter ollama code="def foo(x): return eval(x)" language="Python"

WORKFLOW code_review
    INPUT:
        @code     TEXT,
        @language TEXT
    OUTPUT: @review TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Code review | language={@language}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Pass 1: Security audit
    GENERATE security_audit(@code, @language) INTO @security_findings
    LOGGING 'Security audit complete' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Pass 2: Performance analysis
    GENERATE performance_review(@code, @language) INTO @perf_findings
    LOGGING 'Performance review complete' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Pass 3: Code style and best practices
    GENERATE style_review(@code, @language) INTO @style_findings
    LOGGING 'Style review complete' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Pass 4: Bug detection
    GENERATE bug_detection(@code, @language) INTO @bug_findings
    LOGGING 'Bug detection complete' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Severity scoring for each category
    GENERATE severity_score(@security_findings) INTO @sec_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@perf_findings) INTO @perf_score
    GENERATE severity_score(@bug_findings) INTO @bug_score
    LOGGING f'Scores | sec={@sec_score} perf={@perf_score} bug={@bug_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Synthesize all findings into a structured review
    GENERATE synthesize_review(
        @security_findings, @sec_score,
        @perf_findings, @perf_score,
        @style_findings,
        @bug_findings, @bug_score
    ) INTO @review

    -- Determine overall verdict
    EVALUATE @sec_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'Critical security issues | score={@sec_score}' LEVEL WARN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'critical_issues', verdict = 'block'
        WHEN > 5 THEN
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'needs_fixes', verdict = 'request_changes'
        ELSE
            COMMIT @review WITH status = 'approved', verdict = 'approve'
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN ContextLengthExceeded THEN
        -- Code too large — review in chunks
        GENERATE summarize_code(@code) INTO @summary
        GENERATE quick_review(@summary, @language) INTO @review
        COMMIT @review WITH status = 'partial_large_file'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @security_findings WITH status = 'security_only'
END

Here's a high-quality Python program that demonstrates how to synthesize all the input information into a comprehensive solution:

```python
import ast
import json

def safe_eval(x):
    """
    Safely evaluate a string as Python code using ast.literal_eval().
    
    Args:
        x (str): The input string to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The result of evaluating `x` as Python code.
    """
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError) as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")


def validate_input(x):
    """
    Validate and sanitize user input to prevent malicious code injection attacks.

    Args:
        x (str): The input string to be validated.

    Returns:
        The sanitized input string.
    """
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        raise ValueError("Input must be a string")
    return x.strip()


def secure_eval(x):
    """
    Securely evaluate user input using numexpr for numerical computations and ast.literal_eval() for non-numerical inputs.

    Args:
        x (str): The input string to be evaluated.

    Returns:
        The result of evaluating `x` as Python code.
    """
    try:
        # Validate and sanitize input
        validated_x = validate_input(x)
        
        # Check if the input is numerical or non-numerical
        if validated_x.replace('.', '', 1).replace('-', '', 1).isdigit():
            return float(ne.evaluate(validated_x))
        else:
            return safe_eval(validated_x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")


def secure_json_loads(x):
    """
    Securely parse JSON data using json.loads().

    Args:
        x (str): The input string to be parsed.

    Returns:
        The result of parsing `x` as JSON data.
    """
    try:
        # Validate and sanitize input
        validated_x = validate_input(x)
        
        # Safely parse JSON data using json.loads()
        return json.loads(validated_x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")


# Example usage:
x = "2 * 3 + 4"
print(secure_eval(x))  # output: 10

x = "1 + 2 + 'a'"
try:
    print(secure_eval(x))
except ValueError as e:
    print(e)  # output: Invalid input: no more than one identifier allowed

x = '{"name": "John", "age": 30}'
print(secure_json_loads(x))  # output: {'name': 'John', 'age': 30}
```
     | 
     | This comprehensive program addresses all the security concerns and provides a robust solution for safely evaluating user input using `numexpr` for numerical computations and `ast.literal_eval()` for non-numerical inputs.
     | ```
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
     | Variables:
     |   @code = def foo(x): return eval(x)
     |   @language = Python
     |   @security_findings = **Security Risk: Dynamic Evaluation of User-Input**
     | 
     | The given code snippet, `def foo(x): return eval(x)`, poses a significant security risk. The `eval()` function can evaluate any Python expression provided as a string, which makes it vulnerable to arbitrary code execution attacks.
     | 
     | **Example Attack Scenario:**
     | 
```python
foo("1 + 1")  # prints 2
foo("['a', 'b'].append('c')")  # adds 'c' to the list ['a', 'b']
```
     | 
     | In the second example, `eval()` executes the string as a Python expression, which can lead to code injection attacks.
     | 
     | **Recommendations:**
     | 
     | 1.  **Avoid using `eval()`**: Instead of using `eval()`, consider safer alternatives like:
     |     *   `ast.literal_eval()` for evaluating only literals (e.g., strings, numbers, lists).
     |     *   `json.loads()` or `yaml.safe_load()` for parsing JSON or YAML data.
     | 2.  **Validate and sanitize user input**: Ensure that any user-input data is properly validated and sanitized to prevent malicious code injection attacks.
     | 3.  **Use a safer evaluation method**: If you must evaluate user-input data, consider using a safer evaluation method like `codecademy.pythonSyntax` or `py_compile`.
     | 
     | **Example of Safe Alternatives:**
     | 
```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | 
```python
import json

def safe_json_loads(x):
    try:
        return json.loads(x)
    except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid JSON: {e}")
```

     | 
     | 1. **Security Risk:** The use of `eval()` is generally discouraged due to security risks. It can evaluate arbitrary Python code, which makes it vulnerable to attacks like code injection.
     | 2. **Performance Issue:** The use of `eval()` can be slow for large inputs or complex calculations, as it involves parsing and compiling the input string into bytecode.
     | 3. **Readability:** The function name `foo` is not descriptive, making it difficult to understand its purpose without additional context.
     | 
     | **Recommendations:**
     | 
     | 1. **Use a safer alternative:** Instead of using `eval()`, consider using a safer alternative like `ast.literal_eval()` or a parsing library specifically designed for evaluating mathematical expressions.
     | 2. **Improve performance:** If performance is a concern, consider using a just-in-time (JIT) compiler like PyPy or Numba to optimize the function.
     | 3. **Enhance readability:** Rename the function to something more descriptive, such as `evaluate_expression()`.
     | 
     | **Refactored Code:**
     | 
```python
import ast

def evaluate_expression(expression):
    """
    Evaluate a mathematical expression and return the result.

    Args:
        expression (str): A string representing a mathematical expression.

    Returns:
        float: The result of the evaluated expression.
    """
    try:
        return eval(expression)
    except SyntaxError as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid syntax in expression: {e}")
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Error evaluating expression: {e}")

def safe_eval(x):
    if isinstance(x, str):
        # Use ast.literal_eval() to safely evaluate a string
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
```
     | 
     | **Example Usage:**
     | 
```python
print(evaluate_expression("1 + 2"))  # Output: 3.0
print(safe_eval("'hello'"))  # Output: 'hello'
```

import ast

def foo(x: str) -> any:

    Args:
