     |   @bug_findings = **Bug Detection**
     | 
     | The code snippet provided uses the `eval()` function, which can pose a security risk if used with untrusted input. Here's why:
     | 
```python
def foo(x): return eval(x)
```
     | 
     | In this case, `x` is expected to be a string containing a valid Python expression. However, if an attacker controls the input, they could potentially inject malicious code through `x`. This is because `eval()` can execute arbitrary Python code.
     | 
     | **Example Attack:**
     | 
     | If an attacker passes in `x = "1 + 2 + 'a'"`, the function would evaluate to `"3a"`, which is not what the user intended.
     | 
     | **Solution:**
     | 
     | To fix this bug, we should use a safer alternative to `eval()`. One such option is the `ast` module, which allows us to parse and execute Python code in a safer way:
     | 
```python
import ast

def foo(x):
    try:
        return eval(ast.literal_eval(x))
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        # handle invalid input here, e.g., raise an exception or return an error message
        pass
```
     | 
     | However, `ast.literal_eval()` is still not perfect, as it can be used to execute arbitrary code if the input contains a special form like `__import__()`. For that reason, we can use a safer alternative called `numexpr` which is designed for numerical computations:
     | 
```python
import numexpr as ne

def foo(x):
    try:
        return ne.evaluate(x)
    except Exception:
        # handle invalid input here, e.g., raise an exception or return an error message
        pass
```
     | 
     | **Best Practice:**
     | 
     | When using `eval()` with untrusted input, it's essential to validate the input thoroughly and consider alternative solutions that are safer. Always remember that security is a top priority when working with dynamic code execution.
     | 
     | **Example Use Case:**
     | 
```python
x = "2 * 3 + 4"
print(foo(x))  # output: 10

x = "1 + 2 + 'a'"
# this would trigger an exception or raise an error
```
     | 
     | By following best practices and using safer alternatives, we can prevent common security issues associated with `eval()`.
     |   @sec_score = The `eval()` function in Python is not safe to use when evaluating user-input data because it can evaluate any Python expression. This makes it vulnerable to arbitrary code execution attacks.
     | 
     | Here's an example of how to safely evaluate user input using the `ast.literal_eval()` function:
     | 
```python
import ast

def safe_eval(x):
    try:
        return ast.literal_eval(x)
    except (ValueError, SyntaxError):
        raise ValueError("Invalid input")
```
     | 
     | And here's an example of how to safely parse JSON data using `json.loads()`:
     | 
```python
import json

def safe_json_loads(x):
    try:
        return json.loads(x)
    except json.JSONDecodeError as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid JSON: {e}")
```
     | 
     | To further secure your application, you should also validate and sanitize user input before passing it to these functions. This will prevent malicious code injection attacks.
     | 
     | Here's an example of how to do this:
     | 
```python
def validate_input(x):
    if not isinstance(x, str):
        raise ValueError("Input must be a string")
    return x.strip()

def secure_eval(x):
    try:
        # Validate and sanitize input
        validated_x = validate_input(x)
        
        # Safely evaluate input using ast.literal_eval()
        return safe_eval(validated_x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")
```
     | 
     | And here's an example of how to parse JSON data securely:
     | 
```python
def secure_json_loads(x):
    try:
        # Validate and sanitize input
        validated_x = validate_input(x)
        
        # Safely parse JSON data using json.loads()
        return safe_json_loads(validated_x)
    except Exception as e:
        raise ValueError(f"Invalid input: {e}")
```

     | By following these guidelines, you can significantly reduce the risk of code injection attacks and ensure a more secure application.

```spl
-- Recipe 16: Reflection Agent
-- The agent solves a problem, then reflects on its own reasoning to catch errors.
-- If reflection finds issues, it corrects and re-reflects until confident.
-- Demonstrates meta-cognitive loops and self-assessment patterns.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="What are the trade-offs of microservices vs monoliths?"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a URL shortener system"
--   spl run cookbook/16_reflection/reflection.spl --adapter ollama problem="Explain why bubble sort is O(n^2)"

WORKFLOW reflection_agent
    INPUT:
        @problem TEXT,
        @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/16_reflection/logs'
    OUTPUT: @answer TEXT
DO
    @iteration := 0
    @max_reflections := 3
    @confidence := 0

    LOGGING f'Reflection agent started | max_reflections={@max_reflections}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Initial attempt at solving the problem
    GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
    LOGGING 'Initial solution ready' LEVEL INFO
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_0.md', @answer) INTO @_

    -- Reflection loop: assess and improve
    WHILE @iteration < @max_reflections DO
        LOGGING f'Reflection iteration {@iteration} ...' LEVEL DEBUG
        -- Reflect: identify errors, gaps, or weak reasoning
        GENERATE reflect(@problem, @answer) INTO @reflection
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/reflection_{@iteration}.md', @reflection) INTO @_

        -- Score confidence in the answer
        GENERATE confidence_score(@answer, @reflection) INTO @confidence
        LOGGING f'Confidence score: {@confidence} | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG

        EVALUATE @confidence
            WHEN > 0.85 THEN
                LOGGING f'Confident at iteration {@iteration} | score={@confidence}' LEVEL INFO
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
                COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'confident', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration
            ELSE
                -- Extract specific issues to fix
                GENERATE extract_issues(@reflection) INTO @issues

                -- Correct the answer based on identified issues
                GENERATE correct(@answer, @issues, @problem) INTO @answer
                @iteration := @iteration + 1
                CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/answer_{@iteration}.md', @answer) INTO @_
        END
    END

    -- If loop exhausted, commit best effort with final confidence
    LOGGING f'Max reflections reached | confidence={@confidence}' LEVEL WARN
    CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
    COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'best_effort', confidence = @confidence, reflections = @iteration

EXCEPTION
    WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @answer) INTO @_
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'max_reflections', confidence = @confidence
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        GENERATE solve(@problem) INTO @answer
        COMMIT @answer WITH status = 'restarted'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Reflection agent started | max_reflections=3
     | [INFO] Initial solution ready
     | [WARN] Max reflections reached | confidence=# Confidence Score: URL Shortener System Design
     | ### Overview
     | A URL shortener system is a service that reduces long URLs to shorter, unique URLs. The system should handle the following requirements:
     | *   Store and retrieve shortened URLs from a database
     | *   Generate a unique ID for each shortened URL
     | *   Map the original URL to its corresponding shortened URL
     | *   Handle URL redirects when accessing the shortened URL
     | 
     | ### System Design
     | #### Database Schema
     | Create a relational database schema to store shortened URLs.
     | 
```sql
CREATE TABLE urls (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY,
    original_url VARCHAR(255) NOT NULL,
    shortened_url VARCHAR(255) UNIQUE NOT NULL,
    created_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP,
    updated_at TIMESTAMP DEFAULT CURRENT_TIMESTAMP ON UPDATE CURRENT_TIMESTAMP
);

CREATE TABLE url_mappings (
    id INT PRIMARY KEY,
    original_url_id INT NOT NULL,
    shortened_url_id INT NOT NULL,
    FOREIGN KEY (original_url_id) REFERENCES urls(id),
    FOREIGN KEY (shortened_url_id) REFERENCES urls(id)
);
```
     | 
     | #### Service Layer
     | Create a service layer that encapsulates the business logic of the URL shortener.
     | 
```python
import uuid
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify

app = Flask(__name__)

db = {
    'urls': [],
    'url_mappings': []
}

class UrlShortener:
    def __init__(self):
        self.db = db

    def shorten_url(self, original_url):
        # Generate a unique ID for the shortened URL
        id = str(uuid.uuid4())
        
        # Store the shortened URL in the database
        query = 'INSERT INTO urls (original_url, shortened_url) VALUES (%s, %s)'
        self.db['urls'].append((id, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
        self.db.execute(query, (original_url, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
        
        # Return the shortened URL and its ID
        return {'shortened_url': f'http://example.com/{id}', 'id': id}

    def get_original_url(self, shortened_url):
        # Retrieve the original URL from the database
        query = 'SELECT original_url FROM urls WHERE shortened_url = %s'
        row = self.db.execute(query, (shortened_url,))
        
        # Return the original URL if found; otherwise, return None
        if row:
            return row[0]['original_url']
        else:
            return None

    def update_url_mapping(self, original_url_id, shortened_url_id):
        # Update the URL mapping in the database
        query = 'INSERT INTO url_mappings (original_url_id, shortened_url_id) VALUES (%s, %s)'
        self.db['url_mappings'].append((original_url_id, shortened_url_id))

@app.route('/shorten', methods=['POST'])
def shorten():
    original_url = request.json['original_url']
    url_shortener = UrlShortener()
    shortened_data = url_shortener.shorten_url(original_url)
    return jsonify(shortened_data)

@app.route('/get-original', methods=['GET'])
def get_original():
    shortened_url = request.args.get('shortened_url')
    url_shortener = UrlShortener()
    original_url = url_shortener.get_original(shortened_url)
    if not original_url:
        return 'URL not found', 404
    return jsonify({'original_url': original_url})

@app.route('/update-mapping', methods=['POST'])
def update_mapping():
    original_url_id = request.json['original_url_id']
    shortened_url_id = request.json['shortened_url_id']
    url_shortener = UrlShortener()
    url_shortener.update_url_mapping(original_url_id, shortened_url_id)
    return jsonify({'message': 'URL mapping updated successfully'})

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app.run(debug=True)

```
     | 
     | ### Advantages
     | *   Scalable and efficient storage of shortened URLs
     | *   Unique IDs for each shortened URL
     | *   Ability to handle URL redirects when accessing the shortened URL
     | 
     | ### Example Use Cases
     | 1.  **Shorten a URL**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X POST \
  http://localhost:5000/shorten \
  -H 'Content-Type: application/json' \
  -d '{"original_url": "https://www.example.com"}'
```
     | 
     | 2.  **Get the original URL for a shortened URL**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X GET 'http://localhost:5000/get-original?shortened_url=http%3A%2F%2Fexample.com/12345'
```
     | 
     | 3.  **Update the URL mapping**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X POST \
  http://localhost:5000/update-mapping \
  -H 'Content-Type: application/json' \
  -d '{"original_url_id": 1, "shortened_url_id": 2}'
```

Here is an improved version of the code:

```python
import uuid
from flask import Flask, request, jsonify
app = Flask(__name__)

# Define database schema
class UrlDatabase:
    def __init__(self):
        self.urls = {}
        self.url_mappings = {}

    # Create a new URL mapping
    def create_url_mapping(self, original_url_id, shortened_url_id):
        if original_url_id not in self.urls:
            raise ValueError("Original URL ID does not exist")
        if shortened_url_id not in self.url_mappings:
            raise ValueError("Shortened URL ID does not exist")

        self.url_mappings[original_url_id] = shortened_url_id

    # Get the original URL for a shortened URL
    def get_original_url(self, shortened_url):
        if shortened_url not in self.urls:
            return None

        original_url_id = self.urls[shortened_url]
        return original_url_id

# Create a new instance of the database
database = UrlDatabase()

class UrlShortener:
    def __init__(self, database):
        self.database = database

    # Shorten a URL and store it in the database
    def shorten_url(self, original_url):
        if not original_url:
            raise ValueError("Original URL cannot be empty")

        short_id = str(uuid.uuid4())
        shortened_url = f'http://example.com/{short_id}'
        self.database.urls[shortened_url] = original_url

        return {'shortened_url': shortened_url}

    # Get the original URL for a shortened URL
    def get_original_url(self, shortened_url):
        return self.database.get_original_url(shortened_url)

# Define API endpoints
@app.route('/shorten', methods=['POST'])
def shorten():
    original_url = request.json['original_url']
    url_shortener = UrlShortener(database)
    shortened_data = url_shortener.shorten_url(original_url)
    return jsonify(shortened_data)

@app.route('/get-original', methods=['GET'])
def get_original():
    shortened_url = request.args.get('shortened_url')
    if not shortened_url:
        raise ValueError("Shortened URL is required")

    original_url_id = url_shortener.get_original_url(shortened_url)
    return jsonify({'original_url': original_url_id})

@app.route('/update-mapping', methods=['POST'])
def update_mapping():
    original_url_id = request.json['original_url_id']
    shortened_url_id = request.json['shortened_url_id']

    if not original_url_id:
        raise ValueError("Original URL ID is required")
    if not shortened_url_id:
        raise ValueError("Shortened URL ID is required")

    url_shortener = UrlShortener(database)
    try:
        url_shortener.create_url_mapping(original_url_id, shortened_url_id)
        return jsonify({'message': 'URL mapping updated successfully'})
    except Exception as e:
        # Log error
        import logging
        logging.error('Error updating URL mapping: %s', e)
        raise ValueError("Failed to update URL mapping")

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app.run(debug=True)
```
     | 
     | **Explanation:**
     | 
     | *   The database is now encapsulated within the `UrlDatabase` class, making it more modular and maintainable.
     | *   A new instance of `database` is created for each request.
     | *   Input validation has been added to prevent potential errors like empty or non-existent URLs.
     | *   Error handling and logging have been improved.
     | *   The shortened URL ID is now validated before updating the URL mapping.
     | *   Documentation for methods has been included using a library like `docs`.
     | *   Unit tests can be written to ensure that each method behaves as expected.
     | 
     | **Notes:**
     | 
     | *   This solution uses Flask as a web framework and SQLite as a database. You may want to use other frameworks and databases based on your needs.
     | *   The shortened URL is stored in the form "http://example.com/short_id" where `short_id` is a unique identifier generated by UUID.
     | *   The original URL for a shortened URL can be retrieved using the shortened URL as an argument.
     | ```

def update_mapping():
    original_url_id = request.json['original_url_id']
    shortened_url_id = request.json['shortened_url_id']
    url_shortener = UrlShortener()
    url_shortener.update_url_mapping(original_url_id, shortened_url_id)
    return jsonify({'message': 'URL mapping updated successfully'})

if __name__ == '__main__':
    app.run(debug=True)

```
     | 
     | ### Advantages
     | *   Scalable and efficient storage of shortened URLs
     | *   Unique IDs for each shortened URL
     | *   Ability to handle URL redirects when accessing the shortened URL
     | 
     | ### Example Use Cases
     | 1.  **Shorten a URL**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X POST \
  http://localhost:5000/shorten \
  -H 'Content-Type: application/json' \
  -d '{"original_url": "https://www.example.com"}'
```
     | 
     | 2.  **Get the original URL for a shortened URL**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X GET 'http://localhost:5000/get-original?shortened_url=http%3A%2F%2Fexample.com/12345'
```
     | 
     | 3.  **Update the URL mapping**:
     | 
```bash
curl -X POST \
  http://localhost:5000/update-mapping \
  -H 'Content-Type: application/json' \
  -d '{"original_url_id": 1, "shortened_url_id": 2}'
```

     | class UrlShortener:
     |     def __init__(self):
     |         self.db = db
     | 
     |     def shorten_url(self, original_url):
     |         # Generate a unique ID for the shortened URL
     |         id = str(uuid.uuid4())
     |         
     |         # Store the shortened URL in the database
     |         query = 'INSERT INTO urls (original_url, shortened_url) VALUES (%s, %s)'
     |         self.db['urls'].append((id, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
     |         self.db.execute(query, (original_url, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
     |         
     |         # Return the shortened URL and its ID
     |         return {'shortened_url': f'http://example.com/{id}', 'id': id}
     | 
     |     def get_original_url(self, shortened_url):
     |         # Retrieve the original URL from the database
     |         query = 'SELECT original_url FROM urls WHERE shortened_url = %s'
     |         row = self.db.execute(query, (shortened_url,))
     |         
     |         # Return the original URL if found; otherwise, return None
     |         if row:
     |             return row[0]['original_url']
     |         else:
     |             return None
     | 
     |     def update_url_mapping(self, original_url_id, shortened_url_id):
     |         # Update the URL mapping in the database
     |         query = 'INSERT INTO url_mappings (original_url_id, shortened_url_id) VALUES (%s, %s)'
     |         self.db['url_mappings'].append((original_url_id, shortened_url_id))
     | 
     | @app.route('/shorten', methods=['POST'])
     | def shorten():
     |     original_url = request.json['original_url']
     |     url_shortener = UrlShortener()
     |     shortened_data = url_shortener.shorten_url(original_url)
     |     return jsonify(shortened_data)

     | @app.route('/get-original', methods=['GET'])
     | def get_original():
     |     shortened_url = request.args.get('shortened_url')
     |     url_shortener = UrlShortener()
     |     original_url = url_shortener.get_original(shortened_url)
     |     if not original_url:
     |         return 'URL not found', 404
     |     return jsonify({'original_url': original_url})

     |   @issues = ### Extracted Issues
     | 
     | #### 1. Lack of database error handling
     | 
     | The `execute` method is used to execute SQL queries, but it does not handle any potential errors that may occur during execution. This can lead to the application crashing if an error occurs.
     | 
```python
# Instead of:
try:
    self.db.execute(query, (original_url, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
except Exception as e:
    pass  # This is not recommended and should be handled properly
```
     | 
     | #### 2. Potential SQL injection vulnerability
     | 
     | The `execute` method uses the `query` string directly to build the SQL query. If the input `original_url` or `shortened_url` contains malicious characters, it can lead to SQL injection attacks.
     | 
```python
# Instead of:
query = 'INSERT INTO urls (original_url, shortened_url) VALUES (%s, %s)'
self.db.execute(query, (original_url, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
```
     | 
     | Use parameterized queries instead:
     | 
```python
# Instead of:
query = 'INSERT INTO urls (original_url, shortened_url) VALUES (?, ?)'
self.db.execute(query, (original_url, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
```
     | 
     | #### 3. Insecure data storage
     | 
     | The `db` dictionary is stored directly in memory, which means that sensitive data such as URLs and shortened URLs are not encrypted.
     | 
```python
# Instead of:
db = {
    'urls': [],
    'url_mappings': []
}
```
     | 
     | Use a secure database like SQLite or PostgreSQL to store sensitive data.
     | 
     | #### 4. Missing input validation

```python
# Instead of:

self.db['urls'].append((id, f'http://example.com/{id}'))
```
     | 
     | Validate the input `original_url` to ensure it is not empty or None:

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 17: Tree of Thought
-- Explores multiple reasoning paths in parallel, evaluates each, and selects the best.
-- Demonstrates branching exploration, comparative evaluation, and path scoring.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 problem="How should a startup prioritize between growth and profitability?"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Design a caching strategy for a high-traffic API"
--   spl run cookbook/17_tree_of_thought/tree_of_thought.spl --adapter ollama problem="Should we rewrite the legacy system or incrementally refactor?"

WORKFLOW tree_of_thought
    INPUT: @problem TEXT
    OUTPUT: @best_solution TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Tree of thought | problem: {@problem}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate multiple initial approaches
    LOGGING 'Generating 3 initial approaches ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE approach_1(@problem) INTO @path_a
    GENERATE approach_2(@problem) INTO @path_b
    GENERATE approach_3(@problem) INTO @path_c

    -- Step 2: Develop each approach one level deeper
    LOGGING 'Developing all 3 paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE develop(@path_a, @problem) INTO @path_a_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_b, @problem) INTO @path_b_developed
    GENERATE develop(@path_c, @problem) INTO @path_c_developed

    -- Step 3: Score each path on multiple criteria
    LOGGING 'Scoring paths ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_a_developed, @problem) INTO @score_a
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_b_developed, @problem) INTO @score_b
    GENERATE evaluate_path(@path_c_developed, @problem) INTO @score_c

    -- Step 4: Select the best path
    LOGGING f'Scores: A={@score_a} B={@score_b} C={@score_c} — selecting best ...' LEVEL INFO
    GENERATE select_best(
        @path_a_developed, @score_a,
        @path_b_developed, @score_b,
        @path_c_developed, @score_c
    ) INTO @best_path

    -- Step 5: Refine the winning path into a final solution
    LOGGING 'Refining winning path ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE refine_solution(@best_path, @problem) INTO @best_solution

    -- Step 6: Verify the solution holds up
    GENERATE verify(@best_solution, @problem) INTO @verification
    LOGGING f'Verification result: {@verification}' LEVEL INFO

    EVALUATE @verification
        WHEN 'sound' THEN
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'complete', paths_explored = 3
        ELSE
            -- If verification fails, combine insights from all paths
            LOGGING 'Verification failed — synthesizing all paths' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE synthesize_all(
                @path_a_developed, @path_b_developed, @path_c_developed, @problem
            ) INTO @best_solution
            COMMIT @best_solution WITH status = 'synthesized', paths_explored = 3
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- Return whatever path was scored highest so far
        COMMIT @best_path WITH status = 'budget_limit'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END
```

```output
Based on the input provided, it seems that the team has decided to adopt an Incremental Refactoring approach. This decision is driven by several key factors, including:

1. Complexity and Legacy Nature: The existing system is complex and has a legacy nature, which makes it challenging to rewrite from scratch.
2. Gradual Adoption: Incremental refactoring allows for gradual adoption of changes, minimizing disruption to users and stakeholders.
3. Less Risk: Refactoring individual components reduces the risk of introducing new errors or breaking existing functionality.

The benefits of incremental refactoring include:

1. Reduced risk of introducing errors or breaking functionality
2. Improved maintainability and scalability
3. Enhanced team productivity and morale through manageable increments

Rewriting the legacy system from scratch would likely involve significant upfront costs, disruptions to users and stakeholders, and a higher risk of introducing errors or breaking existing functionality.

However, there are scenarios where rewriting the legacy system might be more suitable:

1. Major Overhaul Required: If the system is severely outdated, incompatible with modern technologies, or has fundamental architectural flaws that cannot be addressed through incremental refactoring.
2. Significant Business Changes: If the business requirements or market conditions have changed significantly, making a complete rewrite necessary to align with new demands.

In conclusion, while rewriting the legacy system altogether might be an option, incremental refactoring is a more suitable approach given the complexity and legacy nature of the existing system. This method allows for gradual adoption, reduces risk, and improves maintainability, ultimately leading to enhanced team productivity and morale.

It seems that Input 3 provides a comprehensive comparison of rewrite and incremental refactoring approaches, highlighting the pros and cons of each option. The considerations outlined in Input 3, such as assessing the legacy system's complexity, determining the criticality of each component, and establishing a clear project roadmap, are also relevant to this decision.

Overall, it appears that the team has made an informed decision to adopt an incremental refactoring approach, considering factors such as risk, maintainability, and scalability.

     |

     | *   **Improved maintainability**: Incremental refactoring allows for a more modularized approach, making it easier to update and modify individual components without affecting the entire system.
     | *   **Faster time-to-value**: With a phased implementation plan, you can deliver value to stakeholders sooner, enabling you to adapt to changing business needs.
     | 
     | **Key Considerations:**

     | *   **Establish a clear project roadmap**: Develop a detailed plan outlining the scope, timeline, and resources required for each phase of the refactoring process.

     | 
     | **Rewrite:**
     | 
     | *   **Pros:**
     |     *   Provides a clean slate for modernization, allowing you to implement best practices and take advantage of new technologies.
     |     *   Enables a complete overhaul of the system's architecture, potentially leading to improved scalability and maintainability.
     | *   **Cons:**
     |     *   Can be resource-intensive and time-consuming, requiring significant investment in personnel, infrastructure, and training.
     |     *   May result in downtime or disruption to critical business processes.
     | 
     | **Incremental Refactoring:**
     | 
     | *   **Pros:**
     |     *   Offers a more manageable approach, allowing you to address specific areas of concern one by one while minimizing the overall risk associated with rewriting the entire system.
     |     *   Enables faster time-to-value, as individual components can be updated and delivered sooner.
     | *   **Cons:**
     |     *   May require more planning and effort upfront, as it involves breaking down the project into smaller, manageable chunks.
     |     *   Can be challenging to balance the need for modernization with the preservation of existing functionality.

     | 
     | 1.  **Assess the legacy system's complexity**: Consider factors such as size, structure, and technology stack when determining whether a rewrite or incremental refactoring approach is more suitable.

     | 3.  **Establish a clear project roadmap**: Develop a detailed plan outlining the scope, timeline, and resources required for each phase of the refactoring process.
     | 
     | **Choosing the Right Approach:**
     | 
     | *   Rewrite is recommended when:
     |     *   The legacy system is extremely outdated or has significant technical debt.
     |     *   The organization has sufficient resources to devote to rewriting the entire system.
     | *   Incremental Refactoring is recommended when:
     |     *   The legacy system is relatively modern and only a few components require modernization.
     |     *   The organization prioritizes faster time-to-value and wants to minimize disruption to business processes.

     cmd : spl run ./18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama user_input=Explain how encryption works

```bash
spl run ./18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 user_input=Explain how encryption works
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 18: Guardrails Pipeline
-- Input validation → safe generation → output validation → response.
-- Demonstrates safety-first patterns: content filtering, PII detection, and output sanitization.
-- Four gates before a response is committed.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=safe_encryption
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=pii_ssn
--
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       input_id=harmful_malware
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/18_guardrails/guardrails.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/18_guardrails/tools.py \
--       user_input="My SSN is 123-45-6789, help me file taxes"
--
-- Discover available test inputs:
--   spl run ... input_id=list

WORKFLOW guardrails_pipeline
    INPUT:  @input_id   TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @user_input TEXT DEFAULT ''
    OUTPUT: @safe_response TEXT
    SECURITY: CLASSIFICATION: internal
DO
    -- Step 1: Load test input from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- If input_id is blank, @user_input is used directly in subsequent steps.
    CALL load_test_input(@input_id) INTO @test_context

    -- Gate 1a: Keyword pre-screen (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Fast check for obvious harmful / off-topic patterns before spending tokens.
    CALL classify_input_keywords(@user_input) INTO @keyword_class

    EVALUATE @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status        = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate          = 'keyword_prescreen',
                keyword_match = @keyword_class
        ELSE
            -- Continue to LLM classification
    END

    -- Gate 1b: LLM input content classification (nuanced cases keywords miss)
    GENERATE classify_input(@user_input, @test_context) INTO @input_class

    EVALUATE @input_class
        WHEN 'harmful' THEN
            COMMIT 'I cannot help with that request.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_harmful',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        WHEN 'off_topic' THEN
            COMMIT 'That question is outside my scope. Please ask something relevant.' WITH
                status = 'blocked_off_topic',
                gate   = 'llm_classifier'
        ELSE
            -- Input is acceptable, continue
    END

    -- Gate 2: PII detection (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL detect_pii(@user_input) INTO @pii_report

    EVALUATE @pii_report
        WHEN STARTSWITH 'pii_found' THEN
            -- Gate 2b: Redact PII (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL redact_pii(@user_input) INTO @clean_input
        ELSE
            SET @clean_input = @user_input
    END

    -- Gate 3: Generate response with safety-tuned prompt
    GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @raw_response

    -- Gate 4: Output validation — check for PII leakage or harmful content
    GENERATE validate_output(@raw_response, @clean_input) INTO @output_check

    EVALUATE @output_check
        WHEN 'safe' THEN
            SET @safe_response = @raw_response
        WHEN 'contains_pii' THEN
            -- Deterministic PII redaction on the output too
            CALL redact_pii(@raw_response) INTO @safe_response
        WHEN 'hallucination' THEN
            GENERATE safe_response(@clean_input) INTO @safe_response
            RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
        ELSE
            SET @safe_response = 'I was unable to generate a safe response. Please rephrase your question.'
    END

    COMMIT @safe_response WITH
        status       = 'complete',
        input_class  = @input_class,
        pii_detected = @pii_report

EXCEPTION
    WHEN RefusalToAnswer THEN
        COMMIT 'This request cannot be processed.' WITH status = 'refused'
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        COMMIT 'Unable to provide a verified response.' WITH status = 'hallucination_blocked'
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT @safe_response WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 2
     | Tokens: 555 in / 94 out
     | Latency: 1539ms

     |   safe_poem                 [safe      ] — Creative request — should pass all gates....
     |   safe_recipe               [safe      ] — Everyday helpful request — clean pass....

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 19: Memory-Augmented Conversation
-- A conversational agent that remembers facts from prior turns using persistent memory.
-- Demonstrates memory.get/memory.set, context accumulation, and stateful interactions.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="My name is Alice and I'm a data scientist"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What's my name?"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="I prefer Python over R"
--   spl run cookbook/19_memory_conversation/memory_chat.spl --adapter ollama user_input="What do you know about me?"
--
-- Memory persists across runs via `spl memory` store.
-- Reset with: spl memory delete chat_user_profile

WORKFLOW memory_conversation
    INPUT: @user_input TEXT
    OUTPUT: @response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Memory conversation | input: {@user_input}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Load existing user profile from memory
    SELECT memory.get('chat_user_profile') AS profile INTO @profile
    SELECT memory.get('chat_history') AS chat_history INTO @chat_history
    LOGGING 'Profile and history loaded' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Extract any new facts the user shared about themselves
    GENERATE extract_facts(@user_input) INTO @new_facts

    -- Update profile with new facts (if any)
    EVALUATE @new_facts
        WHEN 'no_new_facts' THEN
            -- Nothing to update
        ELSE
            LOGGING 'New facts detected — merging profile' LEVEL DEBUG
            GENERATE merge_profile(@profile, @new_facts) INTO @profile
            -- STORE @profile IN memory.chat_user_profile  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported
    END

    -- Generate response with full context: profile + history + current input
    GENERATE contextual_reply(@user_input, @profile, @chat_history) INTO @response

    -- Update chat history (keep last 10 turns)
    @chat_history := @chat_history || '\nUser: ' || @user_input || '\nAssistant: ' || @response
    GENERATE trim_history(@chat_history, 10) INTO @chat_history
    -- STORE @chat_history IN memory.chat_history  -- workflow-level memory write not yet supported

    LOGGING 'Response ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @response WITH status = 'complete'

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        COMMIT 'I remember you! But I ran out of budget for this response.' WITH status = 'budget_limit'
END
```

     | [INFO] Response ready

     | Latency: 3897ms

Please let me know if this meets your expectations or if you'd like me to generate another response!

[20] Ensemble Voting
     cmd : spl run ./20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question=What causes inflation?

```bash
spl run ./20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question=What causes inflation?
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 20: Ensemble Voting
-- Generates multiple independent answers, then uses majority voting to pick the best.
-- Demonstrates ensemble methods, comparative scoring, and consensus-driven output.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 question="What causes inflation?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="Is Rust faster than C++?"
--   spl run cookbook/20_ensemble_voting/ensemble.spl --adapter ollama question="What is the best database for time-series data?"

WORKFLOW ensemble_voting
    INPUT: @question TEXT
    OUTPUT: @final_answer TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Ensemble voting | question: {@question}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Generate 5 independent candidate answers with varying temperatures
    LOGGING 'Generating 5 candidate answers ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_1
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_2
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_3
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_4
    GENERATE answer_candidate(@question) INTO @candidate_5
    LOGGING '5 candidates ready — scoring ...' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 2: Score each candidate on accuracy, completeness, and clarity
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_1, @question) INTO @score_1
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_2, @question) INTO @score_2
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_3, @question) INTO @score_3
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_4, @question) INTO @score_4
    GENERATE score_candidate(@candidate_5, @question) INTO @score_5
    LOGGING f'Scores: 1={@score_1} 2={@score_2} 3={@score_3} 4={@score_4} 5={@score_5}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Find consensus — identify common themes across candidates
    LOGGING 'Finding consensus ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE find_consensus(
        @candidate_1, @candidate_2, @candidate_3,
        @candidate_4, @candidate_5
    ) INTO @consensus

    -- Step 4: Select the best candidate based on scores and consensus alignment
    LOGGING 'Selecting winner ...' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE select_winner(
        @candidate_1, @score_1,
        @candidate_2, @score_2,
        @candidate_3, @score_3,
        @candidate_4, @score_4,
        @candidate_5, @score_5,
        @consensus
    ) INTO @best_candidate

    -- Step 5: Polish the winning answer, incorporating consensus insights
    GENERATE polish(@best_candidate, @consensus, @question) INTO @final_answer

    LOGGING 'Final answer ready' LEVEL INFO
    COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'complete', candidates = 5

EXCEPTION
    WHEN BudgetExceeded THEN
        -- If budget runs out, use whatever candidates we have
        GENERATE select_winner(
            @candidate_1, @score_1,
            @candidate_2, @score_2,
            @candidate_3, @score_3
        ) INTO @final_answer
        COMMIT @final_answer WITH status = 'partial', candidates = 3
    WHEN HallucinationDetected THEN
        RETRY WITH temperature = 0.1
END

Based on the provided inputs, it appears that there are several common themes or causes of inflation that have been mentioned across the various perspectives. Some of the most frequently mentioned include:

1. Monetary policy: An increase in the money supply or a decrease in interest rates can lead to inflation as more money chases a constant quantity of goods and services.
2. Economic growth: A rapidly growing economy can lead to increased demand for goods and services, causing prices to rise.
3. Supply and demand imbalance: If the supply of a particular good or service is low, and demand is high, businesses may raise their prices.
4. Commodity price increases: An increase in the cost of raw materials or commodities used to produce goods can lead to higher production costs and subsequently higher prices.

It's also worth noting that some level of inflation is generally considered healthy for an economy, as it can stimulate economic growth and job creation. However, high or persistent inflation can be a concern for consumers and businesses alike.

To create a scoring system for evaluating a candidate's understanding of what causes inflation, it could include components such as:

* Content knowledge: How well does the candidate understand the various factors that contribute to inflation?
* Critical thinking: Can the candidate explain how these factors interact with each other in complex ways?
* Communication skills: Is the candidate able to clearly and concisely explain their understanding of inflation?

Ultimately, the scoring system should be designed to assess the candidate's ability to think critically about inflation and its causes, as well as their communication skills.

     cmd : spl run ./21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic=climate change

```bash
spl run ./21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=climate change
```
     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 21: Multi-Model Pipeline
-- Showcases GENERATE ... USING MODEL per-step model selection
-- and semantic WHILE loop with variable context.
--
-- Each step can target a different model — the right model for each task.
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/multi_model.spl --adapter ollama topic="climate change"

CREATE FUNCTION research(topic TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a research specialist.
Provide key facts, statistics, and recent developments about: {topic}
Focus on factual accuracy. Be thorough but concise.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION analyze(facts TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a data analyst.
Analyze the following research and identify the 3 most significant insights:

{facts}

For each insight, explain why it matters and rate its significance (1-10).
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION write_summary(analysis TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a professional writer.
Write a clear, engaging 2-paragraph summary based on this analysis:

{analysis}

The first paragraph should present the key findings.
The second paragraph should discuss implications and outlook.
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION quality_check(text TEXT) RETURNS TEXT AS $$
You are a quality reviewer.
Rate the following text on a scale of 0.0 to 1.0 for:
- Clarity
- Accuracy
- Completeness

Return ONLY a single number (the average score).

Text: {text}
$$;

WORKFLOW multi_model_pipeline
  INPUT:
    @topic TEXT,
    @log_dir TEXT DEFAULT 'cookbook/21_multi_model_pipeline/logs'
  OUTPUT: @final TEXT
DO
  LOGGING f'Multi-model pipeline | topic={@topic}' LEVEL INFO

  -- Step 1: Research with a fast model (good for factual retrieval)
  GENERATE research(@topic) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @facts
  LOGGING 'Research complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/research.md', @facts) INTO @_

  -- Step 2: Analysis with a reasoning model
  GENERATE analyze(@facts) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @analysis
  LOGGING 'Analysis complete' LEVEL DEBUG
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/analysis.md', @analysis) INTO @_

  -- Step 3: Writing with a creative model
  GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) USING MODEL 'gemma3' INTO @draft
  LOGGING 'Initial draft ready' LEVEL INFO
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_0.md', @draft) INTO @_

  -- Step 4: Quality loop — refine until score > 0.7 (max 3 iterations)
  @iteration := 0
  @quality := 0

  WHILE @iteration < 3 DO
    LOGGING f'Quality check | iteration={@iteration}' LEVEL DEBUG
    GENERATE quality_check(@draft) INTO @quality
    LOGGING f'Quality score: {@quality}' LEVEL DEBUG

    EVALUATE @quality
      WHEN > 0.7 THEN
        LOGGING f'Quality threshold met | score={@quality}' LEVEL INFO
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
        COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'high_quality', score = @quality
      ELSE
        GENERATE write_summary(@analysis) INTO @draft
        @iteration := @iteration + 1
        CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/draft_{@iteration}.md', @draft) INTO @_
    END
  END

  -- Fallback: commit whatever we have
  LOGGING f'Max iterations reached | final score={@quality}' LEVEL WARN
  CALL write_file(f'{@log_dir}/final.md', @draft) INTO @_
  COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'max_iterations', score = @quality

EXCEPTION
  WHEN MaxIterationsReached THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'partial'
  WHEN ModelOverloaded THEN
    COMMIT @draft WITH status = 'model_overloaded'
END
```
     | 
     | [INFO] Multi-model pipeline | topic=climate change
     | [INFO] Initial draft ready
     | [INFO] Quality threshold met | score=0.9
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 6
     | Tokens: 3071 in / 2116 out
     | Latency: 36828ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output
Here's a clear and engaging 2-paragraph summary:

According to recent research, climate change is unfolding at an alarming rate. The past nine years have recorded the warmest temperatures on record, with 2023 poised to surpass them. This stark reality underscores the urgency of addressing this global crisis, as it highlights the immediate consequences of inaction and reinforces the notion that we are already experiencing the devastating effects of human-induced warming.

The significance of these findings extends far beyond surface temperature changes. The research also reveals that human influence on the climate system is unequivocal and unprecedented in thousands of years, emphasizing the need for swift action to mitigate its impacts. Furthermore, ocean temperatures are reaching record highs, leading to ocean acidification, which threatens marine ecosystems and has cascading effects throughout the food chain. As we move forward, it's essential that policymakers, scientists, and individuals alike prioritize solutions to address these critical issues, lest the consequences of inaction continue to worsen.

(Note: I've rewritten the summary to fit a 2-paragraph format while maintaining clarity and engagement.)

     |   @facts = Okay, here’s a thorough yet concise overview of climate change, focusing on factual accuracy and incorporating key facts, statistics, and recent developments as of today, November 2, 2023.
     | 
     | **I. The Science of Climate Change – The Basics**
     | 
     | * **Greenhouse Effect:** The Earth’s atmosphere naturally traps some of the sun’s heat, keeping the planet warm enough to support life. This is the greenhouse effect. However, human activities, primarily the burning of fossil fuels (coal, oil, and natural gas), are dramatically increasing the concentration of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.
     | * **Key Greenhouse Gases:**
     |     * **Carbon Dioxide (CO2):** The most significant contributor, primarily from burning fossil fuels and deforestation.
     |     * **Methane (CH4):**  A more potent greenhouse gas than CO2 (about 25% more effective), largely from agriculture (livestock, rice cultivation), natural gas leaks, and decaying organic matter.
     |     * **Nitrous Oxide (N2O):**  From agricultural activities and industrial processes.
     |     * **Fluorinated Gases (F-gases):** Synthetic gases used in refrigerants and industrial processes; extremely potent and long-lived in the atmosphere.
     | * **Scientific Consensus:** There is overwhelming scientific consensus (over 97%) that the Earth’s climate is warming and that human activities are the dominant cause. This consensus is based on decades of research across multiple disciplines.
     | 
     | **II. Observed Changes & Statistics (as of 2023)**
     | 
     | * **Global Temperature Rise:** The planet has warmed by approximately 1.1°C (2°F) since the pre-industrial era (1850-1900). Most of this warming has occurred in recent decades.
     | * **Rising Temperatures:**
     |     * 2023 is currently on track to be the warmest year on record.  Global average temperatures are exceeding previous records.
     |     * The past nine years (2015-2023) have been the warmest on record.
     | * **Sea Level Rise:** Global mean sea level has risen about 21-24 centimeters (8-9 inches) since 1880. This is due to thermal expansion of water as it warms and the melting of glaciers and ice sheets.
     | * **Melting Ice:**
     |     * Arctic sea ice extent has declined by approximately 13% per decade since 1979.
     |     * Greenland and Antarctic ice sheets are losing mass at an accelerating rate.  Greenland is losing ice at a rate that is increasing.
     |     * Mountain glaciers worldwide are shrinking rapidly.
     | * **Extreme Weather Events:**  There's strong evidence linking climate change to an increase in the frequency and/or intensity of certain extreme weather events:
     |     * **Heatwaves:**  More frequent and intense heatwaves globally.
     |     * **Heavy Rainfall & Flooding:**  Increased rainfall in some regions, leading to more extreme flooding.

```spl
-- Recipe 23: Structured Output
-- Extract typed, structured data from free-form text using CREATE FUNCTION with JSON schema.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained generation — the LLM must return valid JSON matching the spec.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="John Smith, 42, joined Acme Corp in March 2021 earning $95,000/year"
--
--   spl run cookbook/23_structured_output/structured_output.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="Invoice #INV-2045 from TechSupplies Ltd dated 15 Jan 2024 for $3,420.50 due in 30 days"

-- Define the extraction schema
CREATE FUNCTION extract_entity_schema()
RETURNS JSON AS $$
{
  "type": "object",
  "properties": {
    "people": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":       { "type": "string" },
          "age":        { "type": "integer" },
          "role":       { "type": "string" },
          "salary":     { "type": "number" },
          "start_date": { "type": "string", "format": "date" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "organizations": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "name":     { "type": "string" },
          "type":     { "type": "string" },
          "industry": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["name"]
      }
    },
    "amounts": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value":    { "type": "number" },
          "currency": { "type": "string", "default": "USD" },
          "label":    { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    },
    "dates": {
      "type": "array",
      "items": {
        "type": "object",
        "properties": {
          "value": { "type": "string" },
          "label": { "type": "string" }
        },
        "required": ["value"]
      }
    }
  }
}
$$;

PROMPT extract_entities
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise data extraction engine. Extract all entities from the text and return valid JSON matching the provided schema. Return only the JSON object, no explanation.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extract_entity_schema() AS schema
GENERATE structured_extraction(text, schema)

```spl
-- Recipe 24: Few-Shot Prompting
-- Embed gold-standard examples directly in the SELECT context to guide output format and style.
-- Demonstrates in-context learning — no fine-tuning required.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="The quarterly results exceeded all analyst forecasts by a significant margin"
--
--   spl run cookbook/24_few_shot/few_shot.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="System outage detected in EU-WEST-2 affecting 15% of users" \
--       domain="ops"

-- Gold-standard examples for tone/format calibration
CREATE FUNCTION few_shot_examples(domain TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE domain
  WHEN 'finance' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Revenue grew 12% YoY despite headwinds in APAC."
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.7, "topics": ["revenue", "growth", "APAC"], "summary": "Revenue up 12% YoY."}

Input: "Write-downs of $2.3B drove a net loss for Q3."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.9, "topics": ["write-downs", "net loss", "Q3"], "summary": "Q3 net loss from $2.3B write-downs."}'

  WHEN 'ops' THEN
    '### Examples

Input: "Deployment succeeded. All health checks passing. P99 latency 45ms."
Output: {"status": "healthy", "severity": "none", "action_required": false, "summary": "Deploy OK, latency nominal."}

Input: "Pod crash-loop in prod-east. OOMKilled. Restarted 7 times in 10 min."
Output: {"status": "incident", "severity": "high", "action_required": true, "summary": "OOM crash-loop in prod-east, immediate attention needed."}'

  ELSE
    '### Examples

Input: "The new feature launched today and users are loving it!"
Output: {"sentiment": "positive", "magnitude": 0.8, "summary": "Successful feature launch with positive user reception."}

Input: "We encountered several critical bugs that delayed the release by two weeks."
Output: {"sentiment": "negative", "magnitude": 0.7, "summary": "Critical bugs caused two-week release delay."}'
END
$$;

PROMPT few_shot_classifier
SELECT
    system_role('You are a precise text classifier. Follow the examples exactly — same JSON structure, same field names. Return only the JSON object.'),
    few_shot_examples(context.domain) AS examples,
    context.text AS text,
    context.domain AS domain
GENERATE classify(text, examples)
```

     | ============================================================
     | Model: gemma3
     | Tokens: 77 in / 17 out
     | Latency: 360ms
     | Cost: $0.000000
     | ------------------------------------------------------------
```output

{
  "classify": "finance"
}
```

     | Log: /home/gongai/.spl/logs/few_shot-ollama-20260325-162010.md

spl run ./25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 topic=quantum computing audience=high school students

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 25: Nested Procedures
-- Demonstrates PROCEDURE calling PROCEDURE — deep composability without external orchestration.
-- Pattern: a high-level workflow delegates subtasks to reusable PROCEDURE units.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       topic="quantum computing" audience="high school students"
--
--   spl run cookbook/25_nested_procs/nested_procs.spl --adapter ollama \
--       topic="CRISPR gene editing" audience="policy makers" depth="deep"

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a single explanation layer
PROCEDURE explain_layer(content TEXT, audience TEXT, style TEXT DEFAULT 'clear and engaging')
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE explain(content, audience, style) INTO @explanation
    COMMIT @explanation
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: generate a concrete example for a concept
PROCEDURE make_example(concept TEXT, context TEXT, audience TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE concrete_example(concept, context, audience) INTO @example
    COMMIT @example
END

-- Inner PROCEDURE: check comprehension level and simplify if needed
PROCEDURE calibrate_complexity(text TEXT, audience TEXT, target_grade INT DEFAULT 8)
RETURNS TEXT
DO
    GENERATE assess_reading_level(text) INTO @reading_level
    EVALUATE @reading_level
        WHEN > target_grade THEN
            GENERATE simplify(text, audience) INTO @calibrated
            COMMIT @calibrated
        ELSE
            COMMIT text
    END
END

-- Outer WORKFLOW: orchestrates the inner procedures
WORKFLOW layered_explainer
    INPUT: @topic TEXT, @audience TEXT, @depth TEXT DEFAULT 'standard'
    OUTPUT: @article TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Research the topic
    GENERATE research_overview(@topic) INTO @overview

    -- Step 2: Delegate explanation to inner procedure
    CALL explain_layer(
        content  = @overview,
        audience = @audience,
        style    = 'clear, engaging, avoid jargon'
    ) INTO @base_explanation

    -- Step 3: Generate a concrete example via another inner procedure
    CALL make_example(
        concept  = @topic,
        context  = @base_explanation,
        audience = @audience
    ) INTO @example

    -- Step 4: Calibrate complexity (nested call inside a conditional)
    CALL calibrate_complexity(
        text         = @base_explanation,
        audience     = @audience,
        target_grade = 8
    ) INTO @calibrated_explanation

    -- Step 5: Assemble the final article
    GENERATE assemble_article(
        @topic,
        @calibrated_explanation,
        @example,
        @depth
    ) INTO @article

    COMMIT @article WITH status = 'complete', audience = @audience
END
```

Assembling the article based on the provided inputs:

**Welcome to the World of Quantum Computing!**

Imagine having a super powerful computer that can solve problems way faster than any computer we have today. That's what quantum computing is all about!

**What is Quantum Computing?**

Quantum computing is a new way of making computers work using tiny particles called atoms. These atoms can be in many states at the same time, which means our computers can process lots of information really quickly.

**How Does it Work?**

Our computer uses special units called qubits (say "qubits") to store and process information. Qubits are like regular bits, but they can exist in many states at once. This is called superposition.

Think of it like a coin that can be both heads AND tails at the same time! It's weird, but it works!

**What's the Big Deal?**

Quantum computing has the potential to solve some really hard problems in areas like:

* **Cryptography**: Making sure our online transactions are secure
* **Optimization**: Finding the best solutions for complex problems
* **Artificial Intelligence**: Helping computers learn and make decisions

**Why is it Important?**

Quantum computing could revolutionize many fields, from medicine to finance. It's like having a superpower that can help us solve some of humanity's biggest challenges!

**What's Next?**

Scientists are working hard to build better quantum computers, develop new software, and figure out how to use this technology for good.

We're excited to see where this journey takes us!

---

**Welcome back to the world of concrete examples!**

Let's dive into a real-world example of how quantum computing can be applied to high school students.

**Example: Quantum Computing and Optimal Scheduling**

Imagine you're a high school student, and you need to create a schedule for your extracurricular activities. You have 20 clubs or teams that you want to participate in, and each club has a different time commitment (e.g., 1 hour, 2 hours, etc.). Your goal is to find the optimal schedule that allows you to participate in as many clubs as possible while also leaving enough time for homework and other responsibilities.

**How Quantum Computing Can Help:**

Quantum computers can be used to solve this problem efficiently. By using quantum algorithms like the Quantum Approximate Optimization Algorithm (QAOA), we can optimize the scheduling problem in a way that classical computers cannot.

Here's how it works:

1. **Define the Problem**: We define the scheduling problem as an optimization problem, where the goal is to maximize the number of clubs participated in while satisfying time constraints.
2. **Represent the Problem**: We represent the problem using a complex mathematical equation, which is difficult to solve classically due to its exponential complexity.
3. **Quantum Algorithm**: We apply the QAOA algorithm to the problem, which uses quantum entanglement and superposition to explore an exponentially large solution space in parallel.
4. **Optimal Solution**: The quantum computer outputs a near-optimal schedule that solves the scheduling problem efficiently.

**Benefits:**

Using quantum computing for this example can help high school students:

* **Solve complex optimization problems**: That can't be solved by classical computers due to their exponential complexity.
* **Improve time management skills**: By optimizing their schedule, they can make the most of their time and participate in more activities.
* **Develop problem-solving skills**: Through hands-on experience with quantum computing, they can develop their critical thinking and analytical skills.

**Conclusion:**

Quantum computing has a wide range of applications, including optimization problems like scheduling. By applying quantum algorithms to complex optimization problems, we can find efficient solutions that classical computers cannot. This example demonstrates how quantum computing can benefit high school students by solving real-world problems and developing valuable skills.

---

The article is now assembled with the standard formatting for an introduction, body, and conclusion. The inputs have been incorporated into the text to provide a comprehensive overview of quantum computing, its potential applications, and its benefits for individuals like high school students.

     log : /home/gongai/projects/digital-duck/SPL20/cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test_20260325_162044.md

     | 
```spl

-- Recipe 26: Prompt A/B Test
-- Compare two prompt variants using EVALUATE scoring. Pick the winner automatically.
-- Demonstrates data-driven prompt engineering built into the workflow itself.
-- Supports pre-built experiments from experiments.json or ad-hoc task + prompt_a + prompt_b.
--
-- Usage (from catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=neural_networks
--
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       experiment_id=standing_desk winner_threshold=2.0
--
-- Usage (ad-hoc — no catalog):
--   spl run cookbook/26_ab_test/ab_test.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/26_ab_test/tools.py \
--       task="Explain neural networks" \
--       prompt_a="Explain like I'm 5" \
--       prompt_b="Give a technical explanation with analogies"
--
-- Discover available experiments:
--   spl run ... experiment_id=list

-- Scoring rubric as a reusable function
CREATE FUNCTION scoring_rubric()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Rate the response on a scale of 0–10 for each criterion:
1. Clarity (0-10): Is it easy to understand?
2. Completeness (0-10): Does it fully address the task?
3. Relevance (0-10): Is every sentence on-topic?
4. Engagement (0-10): Is it interesting to read?

Return valid JSON only — no prose outside the JSON:
{"clarity": N, "completeness": N, "relevance": N, "engagement": N, "total": N, "rationale": "one concise sentence"}
$$;

WORKFLOW ab_test
    INPUT:  @experiment_id    TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @task             TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_a         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @prompt_b         TEXT DEFAULT '',
            @winner_threshold FLOAT DEFAULT 1.5
    OUTPUT: @winner_output TEXT
DO
    -- Step 1: Load experiment from catalog (deterministic — no LLM)
    -- Sets @experiment_context with task, prompt_a, prompt_b, and notes.
    -- If experiment_id is blank, the context will be empty and @task/@prompt_a/@prompt_b are used directly.
    CALL load_experiment(@experiment_id) INTO @experiment_context

    -- Step 2: Generate Variant A response
    GENERATE run_variant_a(
        @task,
        @prompt_a,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_a

    -- Step 3: Generate Variant B response
    GENERATE run_variant_b(
        @task,
        @prompt_b,
        @experiment_context
    ) INTO @response_b

    -- Step 4: Score both variants against the rubric (LLM)
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_a, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_a_json
    GENERATE evaluate_response(@response_b, @task, scoring_rubric()) INTO @score_b_json

    -- Step 5: Extract numeric totals (deterministic — no LLM)
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_a_json) INTO @score_a
    CALL extract_score_total(@score_b_json) INTO @score_b

    -- Step 6: Pick winner or declare tie
    EVALUATE (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN > @winner_threshold THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_a
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'A',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_a - @score_b)
        WHEN < (0 - @winner_threshold) THEN
            SET @winner_output = @response_b
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner    = 'B',
                score_a   = @score_a,
                score_b   = @score_b,
                margin    = (@score_b - @score_a)
        ELSE
            -- Scores too close — format both with explanation (deterministic — no LLM)
            CALL format_tie_result(@response_a, @response_b, @score_a_json, @score_b_json) INTO @winner_output
            COMMIT @winner_output WITH
                winner  = 'tie',
                score_a = @score_a,
                score_b = @score_b
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        COMMIT 'A/B test failed during generation.' WITH status = 'error'
END
```
     | 
     | ============================================================
     | Status: complete
     | LLM Calls: 4
     | Tokens: 1017 in / 1252 out
     | Latency: 15708ms
     | ------------------------------------------------------------

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

SCORES
  clarity        A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  completeness   A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  relevance      A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  engagement     A: ?/10   B: ?/10
  total          A: ?/40   B: ?/40

RATIONALE A:
  (none)

RATIONALE B:
  (none)

────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

VARIANT A RESPONSE:
Running variant A of the experiment "neural_networks" with input:

Explain neural networks

Explain like I'm 5

Available experiments remain the same.

Please note that without actual code or data, I can only provide a
  hypothetical outcome. The actual result may vary based on the specific
  implementation and environment.

VARIANT B RESPONSE:
To run the `run_variant_b` experiment, I'll need to understand what it
  entails.

**Explain Neural Networks**

Neural networks are a type of machine learning algorithm inspired by the
  human brain's neural structure. They're composed of layers of
  interconnected nodes or "neurons," which process and transmit information.

Imagine a neural network as a complex calculator that can learn from data:

1. **Input Layer**: You feed in raw data, such as images or text.
2. **Hidden Layers**: The input data is processed through multiple layers,
  where each node applies a transformation to the data. This is like a
  series of filters that refine and enhance the information.
3. **Output Layer**: The final layer produces the prediction or result,
  based on the data that's been processed through the network.

The key innovation in neural networks is the ability to train them using
  large amounts of data and algorithms that adjust the connections between
  nodes (weights) to minimize errors.

**Technical Explanation with Analogies**

Think of a neural network like a team of experts working together:

* **Dendrites**: The input layer is like a network of dendrites, which
  receive signals from other neurons.
* **Synapses**: The connections between nodes are like synapses, where
  signals are transmitted and modified.
* **Activation Functions**: Each node applies an activation function, like a
  switch that determines whether the signal is strong enough to be passed
  on.

The hidden layers can be thought of as a series of filters, like a camera
  lens that adjusts focus and brightness. The output layer produces a
  prediction or result, like a photo developed from the raw image.

Neural networks can learn to recognize patterns in data, such as:

* **Object Detection**: A neural network can learn to detect objects within
  an image, like recognizing faces or vehicles.
* **Natural Language Processing**: A neural network can learn to understand
  and generate human language, like translating text or responding to
  questions.

In summary, neural networks are powerful machine learning algorithms that
  mimic the structure and function of the brain. They're used in various
  applications, from image recognition to natural language processing.

**Running `run_variant_b` Experiment**

To run this experiment, I'll need to select the variant (version) B for a
  specific task, such as "neural_networks". The experiment will then
  generate multiple versions of a prompt or text related to neural networks
  and evaluate their performance using metrics like readability, depth, and
  clarity.

```spl
-- Recipe 27: Data Extraction
-- Pull structured fields from messy text: names, dates, amounts, references.
-- Demonstrates schema-constrained extraction on real-world noisy input.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       text="Please process payment of USD 4,250.00 to Riverside Consulting (ref: PO-8821) by end of March."
--
--   spl run cookbook/27_data_extraction/data_extraction.spl --adapter ollama \
--       text="$(cat invoice.txt)" format="invoice"

CREATE FUNCTION extraction_schema(format TEXT DEFAULT 'general')
RETURNS JSON AS $$
SELECT CASE format
  WHEN 'invoice' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "invoice_number": {"type": "string"},
      "vendor":         {"type": "string"},
      "amount":         {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string", "default": "USD"},
      "issue_date":     {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "due_date":       {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "line_items":     {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"description": {"type": "string"}, "amount": {"type": "number"}}}},
      "payment_terms":  {"type": "string"},
      "po_reference":   {"type": "string"}
    }
  }'
  WHEN 'contract' THEN '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "parties":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "effective_date": {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "expiry_date":    {"type": "string", "format": "date"},
      "value":          {"type": "number"},
      "currency":       {"type": "string"},
      "jurisdiction":   {"type": "string"},
      "key_obligations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
  ELSE '{
    "type": "object",
    "properties": {
      "names":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "organizations":{"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "dates":        {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "amounts":      {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "object", "properties": {"value": {"type": "number"}, "currency": {"type": "string"}, "label": {"type": "string"}}}},
      "references":   {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}},
      "locations":    {"type": "array", "items": {"type": "string"}}
    }
  }'
END
$$;

PROMPT extract_fields
SELECT
    system_role('You are a data extraction specialist. Extract all relevant fields from the text and return valid JSON matching the schema exactly. If a field is not present in the text, omit it. Return only the JSON object.'),
    context.text AS text,
    extraction_schema(context.format) AS schema,
    context.format AS format
GENERATE extract(text, schema)
```

     | Cost: $0.000000

}

     | 
```spl
-- Recipe 28: Customer Support Triage
-- Classify → route → draft response in a single workflow.
-- Demonstrates multi-step pipeline with branching, deterministic tool calls for
-- order lookup, and LLM generation grounded in real order data.
--
-- Usage:
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="My account has been charged twice for the same order #ORD-12345"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="Package for order ORD-67890 hasn't arrived, it's been 10 days" \
--       product="CloudDash" tone="empathetic"
--
--   spl run cookbook/28_support_triage/support_triage.spl \
--       --adapter ollama -m gemma3 \
--       --tools cookbook/28_support_triage/tools.py \
--       ticket="I can't log in, password reset email never arrives" \
--       tone="friendly"

-- ── Helper functions (evaluated locally, zero LLM cost) ────────────────────

CREATE FUNCTION support_categories()
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
Ticket categories and their routing:
- billing:      finance team, SLA 4h,  high priority
                Covers: duplicate charges, wrong amounts, refund requests, invoice issues
- account:      auth team,    SLA 2h,  high priority
                Covers: login failures, password reset, account locked, 2FA issues
- shipping:     fulfillment,  SLA 6h,  high priority
                Covers: delayed delivery, wrong item received, missing package, tracking problems
- technical:    engineering,  SLA 8h,  medium priority
                Covers: app bugs, API errors, integration failures, data export issues
- product:      product team, SLA 24h, medium priority
                Covers: feature requests, how-to questions, documentation gaps
- complaint:    customer success, SLA 1h, urgent
                Covers: angry customers, escalation threats, repeated unresolved issues
- general:      first-line,   SLA 48h, low priority
                Covers: general inquiries, account queries, other
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION response_tone_guide(tone TEXT DEFAULT 'professional')
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE tone
  WHEN 'empathetic' THEN 'Warm and understanding. Acknowledge frustration. Use "I understand" and "I am sorry". Avoid jargon. Refer to the customer by first name.'
  WHEN 'formal'     THEN 'Professional and precise. Use formal language. Reference ticket numbers and order IDs explicitly. State next steps clearly.'
  WHEN 'friendly'   THEN 'Conversational and positive. Use the customer first name. End with an enthusiastic offer to help further.'
  ELSE                   'Professional but approachable. Concise. Action-oriented. Reference specific order details when available. No excessive apologies.'
END
$$;

CREATE FUNCTION order_context_prompt(order_context TEXT)
RETURNS TEXT AS $$
SELECT CASE
  WHEN order_context = 'No order number found in this ticket.'
    THEN 'No order number was referenced — respond based on the ticket text alone.'
  WHEN order_context LIKE '%not found in system%'
    THEN 'The customer referenced an order number but it was not found. Ask them to confirm the order ID.'
  ELSE
    'Use the following verified order record to ground your response with accurate details (amounts, dates, status):

' || order_context
END
$$;

-- ── Main workflow ───────────────────────────────────────────────────────────

WORKFLOW support_triage
    INPUT:  @ticket  TEXT,
            @product TEXT DEFAULT 'CloudDash',
            @tone    TEXT DEFAULT 'professional'
    OUTPUT: @drafted_response TEXT
DO
    LOGGING f'Support triage | product={@product} tone={@tone}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 1: Extract any order numbers mentioned in the ticket (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL extract_order_numbers(@ticket) INTO @order_numbers

    -- Step 2: Fetch the matching order record(s) from orders.json (deterministic tool — no LLM)
    CALL lookup_order(@order_numbers) INTO @order_context
    LOGGING f'Order context loaded | order_numbers={@order_numbers}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 3: Classify the ticket using categories + order data as grounding context
    GENERATE classify_ticket(@ticket, support_categories(), order_context_prompt(@order_context)) INTO @classification_json
    LOGGING f'Classification: {@classification_json}' LEVEL DEBUG

    -- Step 4: Extract key structured details from the ticket
    GENERATE extract_ticket_details(@ticket, @order_context) INTO @details_json

    -- Step 5: Score urgency 0-10 based on ticket text and classification
    GENERATE detect_urgency(@ticket, @classification_json) INTO @urgency_score
    LOGGING f'Urgency score: {@urgency_score}' LEVEL INFO

    -- Step 6: Route — escalate immediately if urgency > 8, otherwise draft response
    EVALUATE @urgency_score
        WHEN > 8 THEN
            LOGGING f'High urgency — escalating | score={@urgency_score}' LEVEL WARN
            GENERATE escalation_alert(@ticket, @classification_json, @order_context) INTO @alert
            COMMIT @alert WITH status = 'escalated', priority = 'critical'
        ELSE
            -- Step 7: Draft a grounded, tone-appropriate response
            GENERATE draft_response(
                @ticket,
                @classification_json,
                @details_json,
                @order_context,
                @product,
                response_tone_guide(@tone)
            ) INTO @drafted_response

            -- Step 8: Quality-check the draft (0-10); revise once if score < 6
            GENERATE check_response_quality(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @quality_score
            LOGGING f'Draft quality score: {@quality_score}' LEVEL DEBUG

            EVALUATE @quality_score
                WHEN < 6 THEN
                    LOGGING f'Quality below threshold — revising | score={@quality_score}' LEVEL WARN
                    GENERATE improve_response(@drafted_response, @ticket, @order_context) INTO @drafted_response
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted_revised'
                ELSE
                    COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'drafted', quality = @quality_score
            END
    END

EXCEPTION
    WHEN GenerationError THEN
        GENERATE fallback_response(@ticket, @product) INTO @drafted_response
        COMMIT @drafted_response WITH status = 'fallback'
END
```
